ii
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This project is a Master’s thesis concerning the automation of the extraction and reloca­
tion of 3D prints from a DLP 3D printer. This is in connection to a soft tooling process,
where the 3D print will have to be moved between different processing stations.
The process, environment, available equipment, and appropriate theory have been exam­
ined. This covers areas of robotics, visual servoing, force/torque control, image analysis,
and simulation. Based on these investigations possible solutions are presented and se­
lected.
The project has required changes to, and development of custom equipment for the ma­
nipulator (UR5e), setting up communication (using ROS), development of control algo­
rithms, and creating a digital twin of the system in Gazebo. Finally, the system has been
tested in simulation and in real settings.
The digital twin is comprised of the laboratory and the manipulator with tools. This has al­
lowed for testing the control algorithms before deployment on the actual system. Making
this digital twin has involved tuning joint controllers for the simulated robot and calcula­
tion of the manipulator kinematics. This includes forward kinematics, inverse kinematics,
velocity kinematics, and inverse velocity kinematics. With these, a twist and position con­
troller have been created and tested.
The actual extraction task has narrow tolerances and therefore high­accuracy positioning
is important. For this visual servoing is a solution. In this project a dynamic image­based
visual servoing approach with an eye­in­hand configuration (single camera) has been
chosen. The foundation and theory for this are described in the rapport together with the
actual implementation and testing.
The relocation task requires firm contact with the environment. Therefore pure position
control is unsuited, as it may lead to unacceptable large forces of interaction. For this
force/torque control is a solution. This project presents a damping (admittance) controller,
based on feedback from a force/torque sensor. The control law results in a velocity ref­
erence for the manipulator velocity controller to follow. The foundation and theory behind
this controller, its implementation, and testing are described in the rapport.
Combined, this results in a visual force/torque hybrid control system that has been tested
and shown to work successfully on the real DLP printer system.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing

==PAGE:10==
1
Introduction
Rapid prototyping (RP) technologies, such as subtractive manufacturing and additive
manufacturing, were first seen in the late 1980s. As the names suggest, these aid in
the quick creation of prototypes by either removing or adding material, until the desired
shapes are met [1]. Depending on the product to be manufactured, these technologies
can be utilized in different ways. One can create prototypes for inspection, allowing for
quick testing and iteration, or even use them for the creation of small batches directly.
Alternatively, they may be used for rapid tooling, where the produced object is used as a
tool for making the actual product, rather than being the product itself [1].
Within rapid tooling different approaches also exist. The two main types are soft tooling
and hard tooling, which is defined by the stiffness of the materials used and the fabrica­
tion method. Within these, two more categories are defined: The direct and the indirect
versions. In the direct method, the tool (mould) is obtained directly from the RP method
e.g. by 3D printing it. In the indirect method, a RP method is often used to create a master
of the product (positive cast model), which may then be used to create a mould from, e.g.
by pouring material around it to create a negative cast model [1].
Hard tooling is often used for larger productions with higher demands, and hence often
result in strong steel moulds. This is opposed to soft tooling, which is often meant for
smaller quantity production. Therefore the mould may be created of different and softer
material [1].
The use of RP technologies facilitates an easy design of complex structures for both hard
and soft rapid tooling, making production both faster and cheaper for small and medium
quantity injection mouldings [1]. This combination makes it a highly interesting research
topic, trying to push the limits of speed, complexity, and precision.
1.1
The DTU Soft Tooling Laboratory
At DTU Mechanical Engineering research is being conducted within the rapid soft tool­
ing process. Here they investigate the possibilities of using polymer moulds for injection
moulding. The polymer moulds are produced using an in­house developed 3D printer,
based on Digital Light Processing (DLP). This is a direct, additive manufacturing process,
where liquid photopolymer is hardened according to a projected UV pattern.
1.1.1
The Soft Tooling Process
The soft tooling process can be seen in figure 1.1. First, the mould must be designed,
e.g. using a CAD program, and then it can be printed with the DLP 3D printer.
The printed mould requires additional post­processing and inspection before it can be
used in the injection mould. The post­processing steps include cleaning, curing, and in­
spection. While cleaning is in general the first step, curing and inspection may be swapped
depending on the material used. Optionally an additional step may take place, consisting
of mechanical machining of the print. This can be before or after curing depending on the
material used, as the part may become too fragile after curing. Lastly, the mould can be
used in the injection moulding machine [2].
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
1
==PAGE:11==
Figure 1.1: The soft tooling process chain. It consists of 4 main phases: Design, printing,
post­processing of the printed mould, and use in the injection mould [2]. These steps
require moving the 3D print.
1.1.2
The Soft Tooling Laboratory
A conceptual overview of the soft tooling laboratory can be seen in figure 1.2. Shown
are the four most important stations, namely the DLP 3D printer, the cleaning station,
the curing station, and the inspection (CMM) station. It is a human working environment
where the stations are located within a few steps from each other. Many of the stations
are human­operated and currently not designed purposefully for automation.
Figure 1.2: The workspace of the soft tool­
ing lab.
Figure 1.3: Flowchart of general the au­
tomation task.
1.2
Automation Objective
The overall vision is to automate as much as possible, of the soft tooling process chain,
related to this specific setup. Doing so may allow for increased efficiency and precision.
Additionally, it may promote a healthier working environment, as contact with the toxic
liquid photopolymer used in the 3D printing process is minimized.
2
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:12==
In between many of the described steps of the soft tooling process, the 3D print has to be
moved from one station to another. These can be considered pick and place tasks, and
this is where the automation may take place.
It is intended to use an UR5e robot arm for this repositioning of the print. Additionally,
it should be mobile, as the distances between the stations are too large for the working
range of the robot arm. It has been discussed to attach the arm onto a mobile platform
for this purpose.
The consequence of this mobility is, that the positions will be slightly uncertain. This
combined with very narrow position tolerances for extraction and relocation makes it a
challenging task to accomplish.
In general, this automation can be considered consisting of two independent and decou­
pled parts, namely the manipulation and the autonomous mobility.
1.2.1
Previous Automation Work
The automation of the soft tooling process has already been initiated by Crone and Lønkjær,
who in their M.Sc. project ”Automating the extraction of a build plate from a DLP 3D
printer” [3] investigated possible solutions for extraction of the 3D print and build plate
using a robot arm. They developed a custom mount and gripper and figure 1.4 shows the
resulting contraption. The two mount parts (A and B) can lock onto each other and hereby
makes it possible to attach and detach the build plate to/from the DLP. When detached,
the gripper (Fork) can lock onto Mount Part B, and move the Print Base around.
Crone and Lønkjær additionally set up a visual servoing system for positioning of the robot
arm and made it all work together. While the project resulted in a functional prototype, it
still mentioned room for improvement of the controller used, especially in terms of speed
[3].
Figure 1.4: The design of the mount, build plate, and 3D print, that the robot will carry.
The Fork on the manipulator end­effector makes it possible to move the print base. It
must be placed with the 3D print facing upwards on the CMM.
1.2.2
Project Scope
Automating the entire process would be a very large task and outside the reasonable lim­
its of this project. Hence the project scope must be narrowed down, although some of the
already developed solutions can be utilized.
As mentioned, the autonomous mobility and the manipulation tasks are independent and
decoupled. The mobility may only concern about moving the robot from initial position to
initial position, while the manipulation part may only consider the extraction and relocation
of the Print Base.
To prove that automation of the soft tooling lab is possible, automating the manipulation
part is most important. Developing, testing, and implementing important features for such
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
3
==PAGE:13==
an automation will therefore be the focus of this project.
Crone and Lønkjær were able to do the extraction of the Print Base autonomously [3],
but have not considered the subsequent task of relocating it. Hence this new task raises
new issues not previously considered for the current setup and solutions. Therefore the
previous solutions will be assessed, evaluated, and possibly improved with the new task
in mind.
Apart from this, the new task is to move the print from the DLP printer to one of the stations
(e.g. the CMM inspection station). This part of the process inflicts more and new restric­
tions on the system. The uncertainties of equipment position, as well as the uncertainties
induced by the arm and manipulator, make a pure position­based control approach un­
suited for the task. This is because small errors may lead to unacceptable large forces of
interaction with the equipment and environment [4], hence these must be confined.
As the curing and cleaning station are currently unsuited for use in relation to this automa­
tion, the CMM instead will be the main goal of relocation. Here the Print Base shall be put
upside down onto the glass plate of the CMM (see figure 1.4), additionally motivating the
limitation of the forces.
In summary, this project will investigate the combined use of visual servoing and force/­
torque control in a hybrid control scheme. The visual servoing presents a possible solution
to high accuracy initial positioning, while force/torque control may ensure careful interac­
tion with the environment.
1.2.3
Task Overview
The control of the robot will be executed in sequences and may be summarized in the
steps of figure 1.3. The robot will move to a, more or less, uncertain initial position at the
DLP. From here visual servoing is used to reach a very accurate position. Then the Print
Base can be extracted. Now two things may happen, depending on the desired workflow:
1. Human interaction can happen, where a person can remove the Print Base from the
robot, clean it, and reinsert it upside down. Then the Print Base may be relocated
onto the CMM using force/torque control.
2. Force/torque control may be used to directly put the Print Base onto a waiting station.
From here the robot can do other tasks, e.g. inserting a new Print Base onto the
DLP printer, hereby allowing for continuous printing.
1.3
Report Overview
The chapters of this report will describe how an automation solution for the given problem
statement has been selected, designed, implemented, and tested.
Chapter 2 will start by describing the base theory and methodology of the subjects in­
volved in this project. Additionally, a brief literature review will be given on the way, de­
scribing related theory, problems and solutions.
Chapter 3 will cover the environment and equipment that is available and lays founda­
tions for this project. Some of the equipment has been modified to accommodate the new
task and these changes are also covered.
Chapter 4 will cover the scene analysis for the specific camera setup, as well as camera
calibration and detection of ArUco markers.
Chapter 5 will cover how the Gazebo simulator has been set up to simulate a digital twin.
This includes calibration of joint controllers, calculation of the robot forward kinematics,
inverse kinematics, velocity kinematics, and inverse velocity kinematics. Testing and ver­
ification of the digital twin are also performed. Lastly, it covers how the communication to
URSim and the real UR5e have been set up, as well as an overview of the general system
communication.
4
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:14==
Chapter 6 will cover the theory of the chosen dynamic image­based visual servoing algo­
rithm and how it has been implemented. Additionally results from testing in the simulator
and on the real system are presented.
Chapter 7 will cover the theory and implementation of the admission force/torque con­
troller. Also here results from testing in the simulator and on the real system are pre­
sented.
Chapter 8 describes a combined test of the entire system, using both visual servoing and
force/torque control in a hybrid control scheme.
Chapter 9 will cover the discussion of potential issues and improvements of the system.
Chapter 10 covers lastly a conclusion summarizing the results and findings of the project.
It is recommended to use the Glossary as reference for common expressions and terms
in this report. In appendix A one can find links to videos of several tests. These videos
are also linked to in the appropriate sections. Appendix B contains the time schedule
(GANTT chart) of this project.

5
==PAGE:15==
2
System and Methodology
A robotic system is highly complex and can take many different shapes and configurations,
even for the same tasks. This is also the case here. The solution presented by [3] present
one solution to the specified problem of extracting the Print Base. Here the task has been
expanded to also relocate the Print Base, and this presents new challenges to be aware
of. Hence the process must be reconsidered and reevaluated to ensure that the chosen
solutions will be viable for the entire process chain of this project.
Therefore this chapter will give an initial introduction to the subjects of visual servoing,
force/torque control, control and communication strategies, and simulation equipment.
Additionally, a short literature review of existing relevant solutions will be presented. All
this shall give an understanding of the possible choices of tools, configurations, methods,
etc. there are available, such that the best possible solutions can be pursued from the
beginning. The deeper theoretical aspects and the actual implementation will then be
presented in the remaining chapters of this report.
2.1
Visual Servoing
The largest limitation of robot systems is caused by the limited sensor capability [5]. With­
out sensors, robots are limited to performing blind tasks with limited knowledge of the en­
vironment, mainly based on design assumptions. Sensors allow for flexibility in the robot
work­space, a flexibility that is very much needed in this case. As the exact positions of
stations in the soft tooling lab are unknown, it is not feasible to rely on assumptions. The
robot must be able to sense where the different stations are located and use this to control
the position.
Here vision is a very useful sensor, as it allows for the detection of objects and for non­
contact measurements of the environment [5].
In this case, the vision will be used for both. First to detect the markers indicating where
a station is, and secondly, to approach the related goal position of the station using that
marker.
Visual servoing spans many fields such as image processing, kinematics, dynamics, con­
trol theory, and real­time and high­speed computing. The goals of the following sections
are to adequately explain the ideas behind the chosen approach.
In visual servoing one should consider two things [6]:
1. The control architecture
2. The camera configuration
Both are independent of each other but may be considered simultaneous. The next sec­
tion will give a brief overview of the two, and lay the basis for review and selection of the
most appropriate approaches.
2.1.1
Control Architectures
Different approaches exist for controlling a vision based­system. A typical approach is
the hierarchical architecture, following a sense, plan, act paradigm [6]. Here control may
happen in open­loop, simply looking (sensing), estimating a goal position (planning), then
moving to it (act). This may not yield the best result, as it depends directly on the initial
accuracy of the visual sensor and the robot end­effector with no ongoing correction. An
alternative to this is to use a closed control loop based on visual feedback, which will
increase the overall accuracy of the system. Using the visual feedback for a position
6
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:16==
controller of the robot end­effector is referred to as visual servoing [5].
To achieve closed­loop feedback two main methods exist [4]:
1. Position­based: Where the position of the goal is estimated and used as feedback
for the reference.
2. Image­based: Where image features are estimated and used as feedback for a set
of reference features.
In general, position­based approaches require reconstruction of the environment and es­
timation of the 3D pose (position and orientation) of the goal in real­time. This has shown
to be a demanding task, that is not always robust [4]. [7] presents a position­based so­
lution for object grasping with a low­cost robotic arm. Here a stereo camera (Kinect) in
global configuration (see section 2.1.2) is used to find features and estimate the features’
3D pose. Using the FLIR camera, that is available to this project, limits the depth estima­
tion methods as a common method like stereo vision is not possible. Usually, for single
camera cases, the 3D pose is estimated from a combination of features and geometric
models [5], one may also consider other depth estimation methods like structure from
motion (SfM) [6]. [8] and [9] present a position­based solution for valve­turning using a
UR5 robot manipulator with a single eye­in­hand camera (see section 2.1.2). They then
use image features to estimate the 3D pose. In general, position­based methods have
been known for requiring too much computational power, affecting the speed of the sys­
tem. Recent years have shown great advances in computational power and methods (e.g.
using Kalman filtering), and therefore this is no longer a great issue. In general, position­
based methods require more prior information (calibration etc.) than image­based, and
the latter is hence more robust in terms of errors [5]. Therefor an Image­based approach
is usually preferred. Here quantities like image coordinates of a significant feature can
be compared to expected values. The project by Crone and Lønkjær [3] used such an
approach where ArUco markers were identified and their outer corners used as features
for the control algorithm. [8] and [9] also present an image­based solution, along with
their position­based, and also concluded that the image­based solution worked better and
more robustly. Based on this, there are no strong arguments to pursue position­based
solutions.
Each of the two methods can additionally take two approaches to the control problem [5]:
1. Dynamic look­and­move: Here the vision system supplies set­point inputs to the
joint­level controller. Hence the internal joint controller feedback is responsible for
stabilizing the system.
2. Direct visual servo: Where the visual servo controller replaces the internal con­
troller, and hereby directly supplies the control signals for the joints. Here the only
feedback is the visual feedback.
The basic principle of both can be seen from the diagram in figure 2.1. Here the red line is
for the direct approach, while the blue is for the dynamic approach. The processing and
the control law are designed depending on what solution is chosen, and if it is position­ or
image­based. In general, the most popular approach is the dynamic, as it simplifies the
control problem, and in general, works better as the internal controller can work with a
much higher frequency than what is generally possible to achieve with the sampling time
of the vision system. Additionally many robot arms, the UR5e included, already have an
interface for Cartesian velocity or position commands, which simplifies the solution and
makes the method more general. Lastly, the method separates the kinematics of the robot
from the visual controller, which allows considering the robot as an ideal Cartesian motion
device [5]. The dynamic approach is also the approach taken by Crone and Lønkjær [3],
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
7
==PAGE:17==
as well as [7], [8], and [9]. Examples of the direct approach can also be found in e.g. [10]
where a robot manipulator and mobile platform are controlled using this.
Figure 2.1: Diagram of direct (Red) and dynamic (Blue) control approaches for visual
servoing. The processing and control law depends on the solution (Red or Blue), and if it
is image or position­based. Based on diagrams from [5].
Investigation of the UR5e robot arm shows, that it may not be possible to bypass the
internal controllers, and therefore the direct control approach may not be a possibility.
Hence using the dynamic control approach will be pursued for this project. The design
and implementation of the visual servoing is covered in chapter 6.
2.1.2
Camera Configurations
Although different types of vision sensors exist, e.g. LiDAR, structured light, stereo cam­
eras, omnidirectional cameras, etc. the most commonly used is still a normal single cam­
era. As mentioned earlier, this is also available for this project. Luckily the single camera
is advantageous as it is well understood and easy to calibrate, compared to many of the
other alternatives [11]. Visual servoing systems usually use one of two camera configu­
rations [4]:
1. Fixed Camera: Also known as global configuration, where the camera has a fixed
position in the system.
2. Eye­in­hand: Or hand­eye, where the camera is mounted on the end­effector.
For the fixed camera configuration, the camera is most often located such that both ma­
nipulator and the object of interest are in the field of view (FoV). Advantages of this are
the constant relationship between the camera and the workspace [4], and that the cam­
era is not obstructing access to the environment. Especially the last advantage could be
favorable for this project. A disadvantage of this approach is that the robot manipulator
may cover important parts of the FoV (occlusion). Especially this can be a problem when
high precision is needed, as it can be difficult to ensure a view of both object and ma­
nipulator at once [4]. Another disadvantage, also especially for this project, is the limited
flexibility of the robot, as one fixed camera most likely cannot cover multiple workstations.
Additionally placing the camera far away from the targets will limit the distance­per­pixel
8
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:18==
resolution significantly. E.g. [7] used a global configuration for a small workspace and
with quite limited precision. These disadvantage makes the fixed camera configuration
unsuited for this case, as the system should be flexible in this regard.
The Eye­in­hand configuration is very common, and is also used in the cases of [3], [8],
[9] and [10]. The camera is usually located above the wrist of the manipulator. Such a
configuration makes it possible for the camera to see both the manipulator and the ob­
ject, while motion of the wrist will not inflict camera motion. As a consequence, there will
not be a constant relationship between the camera and the workspace. Therefore small
changes in position can lead to large changes of the observed scene [4]. This said not all
configurations assume a camera position above the wrist. It is also common to mount the
camera onto the end­effector, hereby assuring a constant relation between the camera
frame and the end­effector frame [5]. The latter is also the case for the solution proposed
by Crone and Lønkjær [3]. For both ways, one must consider the position of the camera,
when moving the manipulator to avoid unintended collisions.
Based on the above, the eye­in­hand configuration is assessed to be most suitable for this
project. The design and implementation are covered in chapter 6. Camera calibration and
feature detection are covered in chapter 4.
2.2
Force/Torque Control
To complete the tasks, it is very important for the robot to sense the unknown environment,
especially when contacts are involved. The environment may set kinematic constraints
to what movements the robot can do [12]. If one does not know the environment exactly
and has to rely on pure position control, one can imagine three scenarios for a rigid robot
contact with a rigid static surface, like the CMM [12] [4]:
1. The robot position is exact, and touches the environment exactly as intended.
2. The robot position is too far away, and hence does not touch the environment at all.
3. The robot position is too close, and as the robot experiences a position error, the
controllers will build up a larger control effort, until either saturation is reached or
something breaks.
The first case is highly unrealistic, as uncertainties are always present. The second case
will lead to failure of the task, as there is no contact. The same goes for the third, which
additionally may have dire consequences for the system.
This case is only for the contact of two rigid bodies. Other tasks often involve movement
(e.g. when polishing or machining), and here uncertainties lead to constrained motion.
One may additionally imagine that the environment is described by other dynamics like
inertial (e.g. pushing an object), dissipative (e.g. sliding on a surface), or elastic (e.g.
contact with soft materials). To make it even more complex, it may be required that the
contact involves specific or limited forces [12].
All such problems and considerations lead to the desire for compliant behavior of robots,
which is often handled by force/torque control.
2.2.1
The Control Task
It is desired to place the Print Base without exerting too large forces onto the environment.
The environment could be the CMM, as illustrated in figure 1.4, or just some waiting sta­
tion. Either way, too large forces may simply damage the glass, Fork, mount, etc. There­
fore the forces must be constrained, to which force/torque control may be a solution.
The Fork has a little flexibility due to the use of plastic materials and its shape. The flex­
ibility is largest at the prong tips. This gives some compliance which is important for the
force/torque control [12].
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
9
==PAGE:19==
One may consider if the visual servoing could be used for this task as well. The visual
servoing is to have a precision of ± 1 mm, and 0.017 rad, in order to extract the Print
Base [3]. It may hence be possible that the compliant structure of the Fork could allow for
precise placement. One could also consider using other surface detection methods, such
as switches (similar to 3D printer endstop), touch sensors, or similar. These possibilities
have not been investigated further.
2.2.2
Passive Interaction Control vs Active Interaction Control
Ensuring the compliant behavior of the robot can be done in either a passive fashion or
an active fashion.
The passive fashion could be structural compliance, where flexibility in the robot and en­
vironment together may be sufficient to accommodate the position errors. An example
here could be the flexibility of the Fork, as mentioned earlier. The Fork may simply bend,
hereby allowing for some position error. Passive compliance can also come from a compli­
ance in the position servo. Here the controllers simply allow for some error in the position,
therefore not building up large forces [12]. This might give larger pose errors. The large
advantage of passive compliance is, that it is fast, as most of it involves mechanics and
not algorithms. One of the disadvantages is the lack of flexibility, as it only handles spe­
cific situations. Additionally, it does not measure the forces, and hence no guarantees on
these can be given [12].
The active interaction control is based on the design of a control system, which can use
force measurements as feedback. Such measurements are often comprised of 3 trans­
lational force components and 3 torques (a wrench). The controller is then designed to
modify the trajectory or forces based on the feedback. As this requires controller calcu­
lations, it is often considered slower, more expensive (need of sensors, computational
power, etc.), and more advanced. As it is slower, the system must to some degree also
have passive compliance (e.g. like the Fork has) [12].
Choosing a purely passive approach would likely require a new design of the end­effector
parts (Fork, mounts, etc.) to increase the compliance. This is not within the scope of this
project. Instead, the approach of this project will be active interaction control, as it also
allows for better control of the forces.
2.2.3
Active Interaction Control Strategies
Within active interaction control two categories can be defined [12]:
1. Direct force control: Here the force feedback loop is closed. Hence one directly
commands the forces of the manipulator. This requires an explicit interaction model,
and the desired motion, forces, and moments must also be specified. Within this
category, some specific methods can be mentioned:
• Hybrid force/motion control, where the force is controlled in the constrained
directions, and motion is controlled in the non­constrained directions.
• Parallel force/position control, where the force and motion control is imple­
mented in parallel, such that force has the highest influence on the output.
This method is often used in more uncertain environments where direct control
is desired [13].
2. Indirect force control: Here the force is controlled via motion control, and the
motion output can be considered a reaction to the felt forces. The contact forces
are usually related to the motion error of the end­effector, through a mechanical
impedance/admittance model with adjustable parameters. This does not require an
explicit model of interaction. Therefore indirect force control usually falls within the
two subcategories:
• Impedance control, where the robot reacts to motion errors by generating forces,
10
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:20==
similar to a mechanical impedance. Therefore it is not a requirement to mea­
sure the forces. E.g. in [14], a UR5 is used for sensorless impedance control, to
circumvent obstacles in a path. Stiffness control is a special case of impedance
control, where the resulting closed­loop system is characterized like a mass­
spring­damper with specified mass, stiffness, and damping.
• Admittance control, here the robot reacts to forces by doing motion deviations,
similar to a mechanical admittance. Within this category, one can find compli­
ance control, which is the admittance equivalent of stiffness control (resulting
in mass­spring­damper behavior). Such strategies have e.g. been used in
[15], where a UR5 equipped with a F/T sensor was tested for use in human­
rehabilitation exercises.
The goal of the control is not to directly control the forces, but rather to ensure that they
stay within some acceptable limits. Although the environment is fairly well known and
might be modeled with high accuracy, this would be a big task, with no guarantees for
improved performance. For these reasons, the direct force control approach has not
been selected.
Instead, looking into the indirect force control approach, one can select between the two
main types. Using impedance control, a F/T sensor is not needed, but it does require
measuring motion errors and commanding forces. This may show difficult, as the UR5e
does not allow for commanding forces or torques, as control is limited to the end­effector
velocities.
Admittance control is, on the other hand, more ideal and straightforward for this task. As
a F/T sensor is available, it is possible to measure the external forces and react to them
through motion (e.g. velocities). Therefore the most ideal choice of control strategy is
admittance control. This choice will lay the foundation for the design in chapter 7.
2.3
Control and Communication: ROS
It is already clear, that the system has many different parts and subsystems that must
communicate and play well together for successful execution. To facilitate exactly that, it
has been decided to use ROS.
ROS stands for Robot Operating System and is an open­source middleware that allows
for a higher abstraction level when designing robotic systems. It makes it possible to
create nodes (submodules) for specific purposes like drivers, control, planning, commu­
nication, etc., and takes care of the internal communication between these [16].
The nodes are, generally speaking, processes that may perform some kind of computa­
tions. These are not limited to one computer, but may be distributed across multiple de­
vices and units. Therefore ROS uses a publisher/subscriber topology, where the nodes
can publish or subscribe to topics. The messages are bound to comply with common
formats, meaning that tools and programs developed in ROS may be reused for different
systems and purposes (e.g. drivers and navigation algorithms).
A ROS master tracks all the elements by registering and naming, and enables the nodes
to find each other. When nodes are combined they form a graph and communicate di­
rectly with one another using the topics and services [16].
2.3.1
ROS in this Project
In this project, ROS delivers a framework for the development of programs and control
algorithms. Here drivers for real robot communication and control are already available.
This makes ROS a very important and useful tool. Additionally ROS can work together
with many different kinds of simulators, which is very useful for fast and secure testing of
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
11
==PAGE:21==
the algorithms.
This project will use ROS, and not ROS2. The UR5e ROS2 robot driver was at project
start only available in a beta version [17]. Hence using ROS2 over ROS was not deemed
worth it, with the risk of bugs and issues related to the beta.
Hence this project will use ROS Melodic, running on a Ubuntu 18.04 distribution, as this
is recommended for the UR5e ROS driver [18].
How the system will be communicating with the chosen simulator and the real robot will
be covered in chapter 5.
2.4
Simulators
Choosing the correct simulation tool is important as it must be able to realistically represent
the task and environment. In this regard, especially the following aspects are important:
• Visual simulation
– Including simulation of normal cameras
• Physics simulation
– Including simulation of force/torque sensors
• Work with ROS
• The UR5e robot should exist for the simulator
The simulator must be able to simulate the visual feedback, as this is used for the visual
servoing. This includes the possibility of simulating the camera as well. The same applies
for the force/torque control, where the simulation of physics and force/torque sensor is
important. Lastly, it is preferable that a model of the UR5e exist for the simulator, although
it might be possible to use another model or set up a simple model manually.
2.4.1
Simulator Selection
Crone and Lønkjær [3] used a combination of URSim and Unity 3D, together with ROS, to
create a realistic simulation of the robot and its environment. URSim is Universal Robots’
robot simulator, that can be used for testing and offline programming with high behavioral
realism. Unity 3D is a game engine that can also be used for robotic simulation by using
adequate plugins. In this setup, URSim created a simulation of the end­effector motion,
that Unity 3D could link to an equivalent camera motion in a simulated environment. This
meant that the robot itself was not represented in Unity, but only the camera. This setup
allowed for a minimum change when going from simulation to the real world, as the robot
IP address only had to be changed [3]. While this is good for a purely position­ and
visual servoing­based solution, it is far from ideal when physics should be simulated.
This is because URSim does not allow for simulating physics, and hence the dynamics
and collisions that should be simulated in force/torque control cannot be implemented. It
could still be possible to use the Unity 3D simulator, as it allows for the import of a robot
model that replaces the URSim. This would require additional packages and work to get
working. Unity 3D especially has its strengths from being based on a game engine. These
strengths are in terms of simulation world size and complexity, where higher photo­realism
can be achieved [19]. Still, due to the use of ArUco markers, photo­realism is not of high
priority, as this is a rather robust solution, that can be tested independently in the real
world.
Despite the above solution may be a possibility, it has not been pursued. Instead, Gazebo
has been chosen. Gazebo fulfills the requirements stated above and is also among the
more popular solutions in both the UR­ and ROS­community. E.g. [8], [9] use Gazebo
for simulation of visual servoing, and [20] also uses Gazebo to simulate a peg­in­hole
task requiring force/torque control. Additionally Gazebo is more well known by the author,
allowing for more focus on the solution implementation.
12
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:22==
Although URSim cannot provide visual or physical simulation, it will still be utilized for
testing the robot and ROS communication, and for verification of velocity and position
controller behavior.
It is worth mentioning that other suited simulation tools exist, but they have not been
chosen either, due to the same reasons as stated above. The interested reader is referred
to [21] for a good simulator comparison.
The setup and testing of the Gazebo simulator and URSim are covered in chapter 5.

==PAGE:23==
3
Equipment and Environment
The following sections will describe the tools and parts that have been used in this project.
The new task of relocating the Print Base raises new physical challenges to the original
design. These challenges will be described, and the necessary changes to the equipment
will be covered.
3.1
Challenges of the New Task
When putting the Print Base onto the CMM it must be put upside down, such that the
print is facing upwards (see figure 1.4). This may be done in two ways, either by having
the Print Base put upside down on the Fork, or by rotating the Fork with the Print Base
180°. By investigating the task and the physical setup, the following challenges have been
identified:
1. CMM Collisions: The Print Base has to be placed upside down on the CMM. This
requires the tool and tool flange to be very close to the CMM. This means that the
following must be considered:
(a) Camera Position: If the entire end­effector is turned upside down, the camera
will be facing downward, blocking any possibility for the end­effector to get
close enough to the CMM to release the Print Base. If the Print Base is turned
upside down, the camera might lose Field of View (FoV).
(b) Camera Mount: The original camera mount has a locking mechanism on the
bottom, that might collide with the CMM when trying to get close.
(c) Manipulator Fork Position: Due to the design of the original UR5e Adapter, the
Fork is placed in the middle of the tool flange, hence it cannot get low enough
to release the Print Base, as the end­effector will collide with the CMM.
(d) Position of the Robot Wrist: The wrist links of the robot may collide with the
CMM if the correct solution to the configuration of the links is not chosen.
2. Fork­Mount Connection:
(a) Fork­Mount Lock: If the original Mount Part B is positioned upside down on the
Fork, the locking introduced by the pegs of the Fork will no longer work. This
means that Mount Part B may slide off the Fork.
(b) Fork Design: The Fork is designed in such a way, that Mount Part B cannot be
inserted onto it upside down.
The first step to the chosen solution is to assume that the Print Base must be removed
and inserted upside down. For this to be possible, the Fork has to be redesigned to allow
for mounting. The Mount Part B should then have additional holes that the Fork pegs
can lock onto, such that it does not slide off when inserted upside down. The UR5e­Fork
Adapter and camera mount must be redesigned, such that the Print Base can be lowered
without end­effector collisions.
These changes will be covered in the following sections.
The position of the manipulator’s wrist(s) may pose problems. Putting the Print Base onto
the CMM requires that the wrists are positioned above the end­effector. To ensure this,
the controller must choose the correct configuration of the joints. This is an important
issue to be aware of.
14
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:24==
3.2
The UR5e
The UR5e is a medium­sized robot from Universal Robots’ e­series and can be seen in
figure 3.1. It has a work range of 850 mm and a load capacity of 5 kg. This makes it
ideal for low­weight process tasks just like the one intended for this project. It has a total
of 6 revolute joints (6 DOF), all capable of doing a full rotation of 360°, at a maximum
speed of 180 °/s [22]. Such 6­DOF robot arms are often referred to as general purpose
manipulators, as it has the minimum number of joints allowing for complete control of the
end­effector pose (position and orientation) [23]. Precision wise it has a Pose Repeata­
bility (PR) (ISO 9283) of ±0.03 mm [22]. Say one has a programmed position of the tool
center point (TCP), that the robot arm should repeatedly return to, then RP indicates at
what precision one can achieve. The calculations of RP according to ISO 9283 are based
on an ISO cube describing the usual workspace of the robot. There are 5 points in this
cube, and repeated movements to these are described. Here it is worth noting that the test
is performed in a one­way manner, entering the points from the same direction always.
This gives better results compared to Bidirectional or multidirectional (entering the desired
TCP from multiple directions) [24]. Overall this precision should be more than sufficient
for the tasks at hand. Universal Robots advertise their e­series robots to be cobots, meant
for human­machine collaboration. In this regard, the robots have been certified by TÜV
NORD, in multiple (17) areas of safety features. Among these are limits to the positions,
forces, power, and speeds [25]. These are all features to be aware of when designing
and testing the system, as they pose fair limits to the performance of the robot.
Figure 3.1: Image of UR5e with custom parts. Shown are the joint names. Based on [26].
3.3
Custom Parts of the System
The system uses a modified version of the detachable build plate system, that was orig­
inally designed by Crone and Lønkjær [3]. It consists of parts for both mounting and
manipulation. The Mount Parts are here referred to as Mount Part A and B (see figure
1.4), while the manipulator parts are referred to as the Manipulator Fork (or just Fork) and
the UR5e Adapter. The images of the original parts can be seen in appendix C, while the
new modified parts can be seen in figures 3.2, 3.3, and 3.4.
The system also uses a camera mount for the end­effector, and a fake model of an FT300­
S force/torque sensor.
A brief overview of their design, function, and modifications are given in the following.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
15
==PAGE:25==
3.3.1
Mount Part A
Mount Part A is connected to the DLP printer using screws. It consists of a 3D printed
model, two switches, guides, and an electromagnet. The electromagnet consists of a per­
manent magnet that is always active and an electromagnet that can be activated to cancel
the magnetic field of the permanent magnet, thereby turning the magnet off. The final pro­
totype, as designed by Crone and Lønkjær [3], can be seen in figure 3.2 (a). When put
together with Mount Part B, the magnetic field and the guides will keep the parts locked
together, as long as the electromagnet is not active. When extracting the Print Base, the
Manipulator Fork will move through the guides of Mount Part B and activate the switches
thereby powering the electromagnet. Hence, if both switches are activated the magnetic
field will disappear and drop Mount Part B onto the Manipulator Fork.
Both the 3D CAD drawings and the physical prototype have been available for this project.
The design has not shown to be conflicting with the design requirements of this project,
and hence this part has not been modified. This said, it has shown difficult to activate the
buttons, even when holding the Fork manually, and hence a better solution for disabling
the magnet may eventually be desirable.
3.3.2
Mount Part B
Mount Part B is connected to the build plate using screws. The prototype designed by
Crone and Lønkjær [3], can be seen in figure C.1 (b) of appendix C. The final modified
version can be seen in figure 3.2 (b). It consists of a 3D model and a metal plate with
guide holes. The image shows a 3D printed temporary test solution instead of the metal
plate, as the metal plate is part of the default DLP printer setup. The metal plate locks
to the electromagnet. It is designed such that the Fork can slide into the guides. It has
a rectangular opening on the top, which allows the peg of the Fork to fall in place due to
gravity. This prevents the mount from sliding off when moving it.
The original design has not shown to be optimal for the tasks of this project, and it has
hence been edited: Additional rectangular locking holes have been added, mirrored to
the existing ones. This ensures that the Fork can lock onto the part unaffected if it is
positioned upright or upside down. Additionally, some of the guides have been removed
at the switch end, to avoid them getting stuck on the switches when detaching. For the
finished part in figure 3.2 (b) the guide ends have simply been cut off manually, as it was
easier than printing a new part. The working drawings can be found in appendix D.1.
3.3.3
Manipulator Fork
The Manipulator Fork is a two­pronged fork tool, used as the tip of the end­effector. It
has been designed together with Mount Part A and B, allowing it to slide into Mount Part
B. Here it can disable the magnet by pushing the switches and lock onto Mount Part B
using the pegs. From here the entire Print Base can be moved around by the robot arm.
The original prototype can be seen in appendix C, figure C.2 (a). The manipulator fork
has been redesigned, as it could not slide into Mount Part B when this was turned upside
down. The new design has fixed this and fits with the new adapter. An image of the
finished part can be seen in figure 3.3 (a), and the working drawings can be found in
appendix D.2.
3.3.4
UR5e Adapter
The UR5e adapter connects the manipulator Fork and the UR5e. Two screws hold the
Fork and the adapter together, and additionally, four screws are used to attach the adapter
to the UR5e tool flange. The original prototype can be seen in figure C.2 (b) of appendix
C. This part also had to be modified, such that the Fork can be lowered down to a surface
without end­effector collisions.
16
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:26==
The new adapter ensures exactly that. It has been achieved by simply moving the adapter
part down to the periphery of the base cylinder. An image of the finished part can be seen
on figure 3.3 (b), and the working drawings can be found in appendix D.3.
3.3.5
FT300 (Fake)
The plan was to use a force/torque sensor (FT300­S), but this was not available during
most of the project. Hence some of the tests have been performed without it. To make the
setup as realistic as possible, a new part, the FT300 Fake, has been designed based on
the dimensions of the actual FT300­S. This part can be put between the UR5e Adapter
and the Fork (similar to the real sensor). An image of the finished part can be seen in
figure 3.4 (a), and the working drawings can be found in appendix D.4.
3.3.6
Camera Mount
The locking mechanism of the old camera mount made it impossible for the end­effector to
relocate the Print Base onto a surface without collisions. Therefore a new camera mount
has been designed. Here the locking mechanism has been removed from the bottom to
the sides, and the thickness of the buckle has been significantly reduced. Additionally,
the mount has been designed with a small hole that fits the peg of the UR5e tool flange,
hereby firmly locking the camera’s position. The new camera mount can be seen in figure
3.4 (b). The working drawings can be found in appendix D.5. The original part can be
seen in figure C.1 (a) of appendix C.
3.3.7
Making the New and Edited Parts
The new/edited parts can be seen in the images of figures 3.2 (b), 3.3, and 3.4. All parts
have been designed using SolidWorks. From SolidWorks the CAD models can be saved
as STL files, that, using Ultimaker Cura, can be converted into G­code that a printer may
use. For printing, an Ultimaker 3 Extended (for the black prints) and an Ender v3 Pro (for
the orange prints) have been used.

3.4
FLIR Camera & Lens
The camera available is a FLIR Blackfly color camera (BFS­U3­31S4C­C). Among the
camera’s most impotent features can be mentioned a resolution of 2.048 x 1.536 pixels,
a sensor size of 1/1.8”, and a USB 3.1 connection for fast data transfer [27].
The camera comes without a lens, and therefore a TECHSPEC C Series fixed focal length
lens is mounted onto it. The lens is compatible with the sensor size, which gives an
angular field of view (AFoV) in the horizontal direction of 62.1° and 47.7° in the vertical
direction [28].
(a) The FLIR Camera without Lens. Source:
[29]
(b) The camera lens build up. Source: [28]
Figure 3.5: The camera and the lens.
3.5
ArUco Markers
ArUco markers have previously been used as fiducial markers when navigating towards
the initial positioning before extracting the Print Base. This is a good choice, as it does
not only allow for referencing, but also for identification, which is important in this project.
ArUco markers are a type of binary marker somewhat similar to QR codes (e.g. see figure
4.7). The corners of the marker alone are enough to determine a camera position, given
that the physical size of the marker is known. Additionally, they are robust, as the inner
code allows not only for identification but also for error detection and correction [30].
Lastly, the markers can be used with OpenCV, which is another benefit [30]. More about
the marks and how the detection works will be covered in chapter 4.
3.6
Important Stations
As mentioned earlier, the soft tooling process is composed of many steps of processing,
each at a specified station. This project focus on mainly two stations. The DLP 3D printer
station, where the Print Base is to be extracted from, and the CMM station, where the
Print Base is to be located for inspection. Images of the stations can be seen in figure
3.6.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
19
==PAGE:29==
(a) The DLP 3D printer station.
Here the
mounting parts have not been inserted yet.
The Mount Parts will replace the two middle
metal plates.
(b) The CMM station. The final goal will be
for the robot to put the Print Base onto the
glass plate.
Figure 3.6: Two very important soft tooling stations are the DLP 3D printer station and the
CMM station. The ArUco makers have not been mounted onto the mashines here.
3.6.1
DLP 3D Printer Station
The DLP printer lowers the build plate down into the resin basin. From below a structured
light pattern will be projected onto the build plate, making the resin solidify. Then the build
plate will be elevated a bit and the process starts over. In the end, a 3D print will be
attached to the build plate.
When printing has finished, the printer will move into a waiting position, from where the
robot is to extract the Print Base.
3.6.2
CMM Station
The CMM station is used for post­inspection of the 3D print. The CMM is an optical
coordinate measuring machine (CMM), and hence no contact is needed. The print simply
has to be placed, facing upward on the glass plate. The glass plate allows for use of
backlighting, which is not relevant in this case. Above the glass plate, the measuring
camera is located.
The glass plate can be moved around to ensure the object of interest is in the Field of
View (FoV). This is not an automatic process, and hence to ensure a better automated
soft tooling process, it is of interest to place the Print Base at the exact same location
every time, such that the glass plate should not be moved manually.
20
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:30==
4
Camera Setup
The camera configuration was determined to be eye­in­hand, as described in section
2.1.2. With the camera location and the task of the camera determined it is now important
to analyze the camera setup. This includes an analysis of the scene, the environment,
and the camera used. This will lay a foundation for a better understanding of how one
can expect to control the robot arm, and also make it possible to calibrate the camera for
optimized performance and ArUco marker detection. This is the aim of the sections of this
chapter.
4.1
Lens Properties
The chosen lens is a fixed focal length lens. This means that the focal length is fixed, but
not that the focus is fixed [31]. Hence the focus may be adjusted for this task. In this case,
very precise positioning is wanted, and this depends on how well the corners of the ArUco
markers can be tracked. The final precision will be impacted if the lens provides images
that are out of focus when close to the marker. Hence the focus should be adjusted to
provide sharp closeup images of the markers.
Setting the focus is a balance, as the long­distance images must be sharp enough for
robust ArUco marker detection and reading.
From figure 3.5 one can see, that there is an option for iris adjustment. This can be used
to adjust the amount of light to the camera sensor. As the amount of light is relatively con­
stant in the laboratory environment, this can also be adjusted once, to give well­exposed
images.
4.2
Scene Analysis
When working with camera systems, the scene analysis is important. This is to get a
correct comprehension of both the camera system and the environment that the system
is intended to observe. In this project the camera system is intended for two tasks:
1. Finding ArUco markers.
2. Using visual servoing for initial positioning.
The ArUco markers work as reference points, which have to be found. After this, the goal
of the visual servoing is to get the end­effector into an initial position relative to the target.
Some visual servoing algorithms require both the end­effector and the target to be ob­
servable. This is mostly if the camera is located above the wrist, as the rotation of the tool
then can be observed. Such a method is often referred to as endpoint closed­loop (ECL).
If the end­effector is not observed, it is often referred to as endpoint open­loop (EOL) [5].
In this case, the camera is located below the wrist and hence rotates with the tool. This
means that the position of the tool may be considered observed without explicitly doing
so, and therefore it can be classified as an ECL system.
The following will investigate the consequences of modifying the end­effector tools, to al­
low for better performance in the relocation tasks. As the tool is not observed, this mainly
includes analyzing the field of view in the worst­case scenario, namely when the Print
Base is inserted upside down on the Fork.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
21
==PAGE:31==

4.2.2
Field of View
It is interesting to consider the FoV of the camera in the working distance. As noted earlier
the working range of the UR5e is 850 mm. Using this information can give the actual FoV,
and hereby knowledge about how to search meaningfully for the ArUco markers.
The AFoV in the vertical direction is 33.8°. As more than two­thirds of the AFoV is pointed
upwards, the main part of the FoV will be observing a plane above the end­effector (and
camera). Differently for the horizontal case, where the AFoV is equally observing both
sides with about 31.1°. Using this and the working range, the actual size of the Search
Plane (SP) can be calculated using the trigonometry relations of a right­angled triangle:
a = tan(α) · b
(4.1)
With a being the height of the triangle, b the baseline and α the angle. Here b could be
considered the working distance of 850 mm minus the offset of the camera (45 mm), and
α the split FoV (see figure 4.3). Hence the SP for the vertical direction would be 497 mm,
and for the horizontal direction 969 mm. Hence about a 0.5x1 meter view with Print Base
and 0.75x1 meter without it.
Assuming that the robot is positioned relatively precisely in front of the station, knowing
its position and orientation, this FoV should be enough to find the ArUco marker with­
out the need for a sweeping search. This said it may be desirable to lower the working
distance to increase robustness and minimize the chance of entering singularities of the
robot position.

4.3
Camera Calibration
The precision of the system is very dependent on the camera and the images that it
takes. Therefore even small distortions can have an impact on the performance of the
system. All cameras have some distortion and the FLIR camera chosen for this project
is no exception. Fortunately, it is possible to calibrate the camera such that the distortion
can be removed. To do this, it is important to have some understanding of the camera
and how it works.
4.3.1
Perspective Projections
Understanding how the perspective projection works for the camera is the first step to
understand the distortions. Starting with the most basic camera model, namely the pinhole
model as can be seen in figure 4.4 for both the 2D and 3D cases. Here f is the focal length,
z is the distance to a point of interest, OI is the origin of the image plane and OC is the
origin of the camera frame [6].
It is important to note that in figure 4.4 (b), the image plane origin OI has been moved in
front of the camera origin OC such that the orientation of the actual projected image can
be ignored [6].
Figure 4.4: (a) the pinhole camera model. (b) the pinhole model for 3 dimensions. Here
the image plane origin OI is located between the camera origin OC and the object to
preserve the orientation [6]. Modified from source: [6].
Using figure 4.4, we can denote a spatial point in the camera frame PC = (x, y, z)T , and
its projection onto the image plane PI = (u, v)T . The geometrical relation between these
two points are called the perspective projection and can be written as [6]:

The above model is very important in the understanding of the camera calibration process,
but still, it does not account for distortions as it assumes linear projections. This is not the
case, and it will be considered in section 4.3.2.
4.3.2
Image Distortions
The general perspective projection as shown in equation 4.9 does, as mentioned, assume
linear projections. This means that straight lines in the real world also will show as straight
lines on the image. For most cameras, this is not true. Instead, the lines become curved,
similar to the extreme case of the fisheye camera. This is also the case for the FLIR
camera as can be seen in figure 4.5. Different types of distortions exist. If the pattern
curve inwards compared to the straight red line, it is called a pincushion distortion, and if
it curves outwards, which is the case here, it is called a barrel distortion [6]. Other and
more advanced distortions exist, but are not relevant here, as they usually are used with
more complex lenses at a very wide angle [6].
(a) Full size image of the calibration target.
(b) Same as (a) but cropped and zoomed.
Figure 4.5: Images of the calibration target taken by the FLIR camera at the DTU soft
tooling. A straight red line has been superimposed from corner to corner to show radial
lens distortion, here the type is barrel.
Assuming the simpler model, the following quadratic model can be used to describe the
distortion [6]:
[ud
vd
]
= (1 + k1r2)
[u −u0
v −v0
]
+
[u0
v0
]
with
r2 = (u −u0)2 + (v −v0)2
(4.10)
ud and vd are the distorted coordinates and can be found from the ideal linear coordinates
u and v. Additionally we have the radial distortion parameter k1 which can be found when
doing a calibration.
Meanwhile, OpenCV uses a more advanced model to describe the distortions. Here the
radial distortion can be described as [32]:
ud = u(1 + k1r2 + k2r4 + k3r6)
vd = v(1 + k1r2 + k2r4 + k3r6)
(4.11)
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
27
==PAGE:37==
Additionally, also tangential distortion is considered [32]:
ud = u + [2p1uv + p2(r2 + 2u2)]
vd = v + [p1(r2 + 2v2) + 2p2uv]
(4.12)
Combining these distortions result in an inverse Brown­Conrady model [33]. This is used
for the camera calibration in OpenCV [34]:
ud = u(1 + k1r2 + k2r4 + k3r6) + [2p1uv + p2(r2 + 2u2)]
vd = v(1 + k1r2 + k2r4 + k3r6) + [p1(r2 + 2v2) + 2p2uv]
(4.13)
Here the radial distortion coefficients are k1, k2, and k3, and the tangential distortion co­
efficients are p1 and p2.
This model is sufficient for finding the distortion coefficients for the FLIR camera. OpenCV
does provide the option for calibrating with an even more advanced model, using more
radial distortion coefficients and thin prism distortions coefficients [34], but this is too ex­
tensive for the simpler FLIR camera distortion.
4.3.3
Automatic Camera Calibration using OpenCV
The goal of camera calibration is to estimate the intrinsic and extrinsic parameters of the
camera as accurately as possible. The parameters describe the relationship between the
real­world (Pw) and the resulting image (PI). Hence these parameters may be isolated
and found if one can acquire enough knowledge of real world points and the resulting
image points.
OpenCV uses one of the most popular methods for this, based on Zhang’s method [35].
The method uses a well­known static calibration object (often a planar grid or checker­
board), from which it is easy to extract features automatically (e.g. corners). The position
of these acquired image features may then be compared to the well­defined pattern of the
real world, and hence the parameters can be found.
For this to work, multiple images of the calibration object must be taken, covering as much
of the FoV as possible with varied distance, rotation, and orientation. This ensures that
the data collected is independent and hence can be used to find the parameters [6].
Many toolboxes use Zhang’s method for camera calibration, including OpenCV. Here the
most common calibration pattern is the Checkerboard (while others exist). One has to
specify the number of checkerboard corners (which can be counted as inner white cor­
ners), and an array specifying the 3D point position of the checkerboard corners. This can
easily be done as the pattern is known, and the position can be assumed. This method
does in principle assume that the checkerboard pose is static, but in fact, it does not make
a difference whether the checkerboard is moved or the camera is moved.
One can then load each image, finding the 2D position of the checkerboard corners using
cv2.findChessboardCorners(...) (in Python). To get better results one may consider
finding a better estimate of the corner positions which can be done using
cv2.cornerSubPix(...). When all corners (real world and image) have been found and
added to the arrays, these may be used to perform the actual camera calibration using
cv2.calibrateCamera(...). The function returns both the camera matrix A, the distor­
tion coefficients k1, k2, p1, p2, and k3 (in that order), as well as the rotation and translation
vector for each of the images used. Only the camera matrix and the distortion coefficients
are needed to undistort the images, which can be done using cv2.undistort(...).
28
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:38==
4.4
Calibrating the FLIR Camera
The calibration pattern chosen for the calibration of the FLIR camera is a checkerboard
pattern with 8x10 fields, resulting in 7x9 internal white corners, as shown in figure 4.5.
The pattern has been printed on A4 paper, resulting in a square size of 20x20mm.
The pattern must be planar, and hence the paper must be attached to a rigid surface as
the paper otherwise curves too easily. Here a piece of laminate has been used.
As mentioned earlier, the calibration data must be varied in terms of covering the FoV,
distance, orientation, and rotation. This has been accounted for in the data set created.
Additionally, the calibration will get more accurate the more images are used [6]. Here a
total of 42 images have been taken for calibration.
Using this image data, the camera calibration is performed by following the procedure in
appendix E.1. This is based on the methods described in the previous section 4.3.3. Here
guidelines for installing the camera drivers for this system, and how to use them are also
given. The actual Python script code can be seen in appendix E.2.
Additionally, a python library (FLIRcom.py) has been created with functions for getting im­
ages from the FLIR camera when using the control scripts. This can be found in appendix
I.1.1.
4.4.1
Applying Calibration to Image
Applying the acquired calibration images in the calibration script yields the following cali­
bration parameters:
Camera matrix :


Distortion Coefficients :
k1 = −0.18648111, k2 = 0.15826721, k3 = −0.08864989,
p1 = 0.0016838, p2 = −0.0016133
(4.14)
Comparing the camera matrix with the one OpenCV uses as template (see equation 4.7),
one can see that the values fit quite nicely. This is as the focal length is measured in
pixels. As the pixel size is informed to be 3.45 x 3.45 µm [27], one can find the focal
length by multiplying:
f = 1830 · 3.45µm = 0.0063 m = 6.3mm
(4.15)
The data sheet of the lens specifies the focal length to be 6 mm [28]. Similarly can be
done for the offset of the image plane, which is about 3.4 mm and 2.7 mm.
Applying the camera matrix and the distortion coefficients on a test image, using the
cv2.undistort(...), yields the result that can be seen in figure 4.6. Here the barrel
distortion is removed, and the lines are straightened out.
The distortion coefficients and the camera matrix can be saved as constant variables in
the program when found, as they do not change.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
29
==PAGE:39==
(a) Original.
(b) Undistorted version of (a).
Figure 4.6: The first image show significant barrel distortions, while these distortions are
removed in the second image, using the found calibration parameters.
4.5
ArUco Marker Detection
Detecting and localizing the ArUco markers are the main purposes of the camera system.
This can also be done using OpenCV, which has a set of methods specially designed for
ArUco marker detection and reading.
4.5.1
ArUco Marker Detection in OpenCV
The OpenCV ArUco marker detection is very robust due to the methods it is based on. In
OpenCV one simply has to set some settings and then use the function
cv2.aruco.detectMarkers(...), providing the image in which the ArUco marker might
be found. This function uses the following steps to detect the marker [30]:
1. Adaptive thresholding is the first step performed. This result in a purely black/white
image, where the threshold for either value is determined based on the area of the
image it is applied to. This will make the ArUco marker highly visible due to the large
contrast in the pattern.
2. Finding marker candidates is done using a polygonal approximation, to find the
candidates that are most square­like. This also uses the parameters
minMarkerPerimeterRate and maxMarkerPerimeterRate, which describe the min­
imum and maximum size of the candidates to be found. Especially the minimum
parameter can be important for the time­performance of the system, as too many
candidates will be investigated if set too low. The default value for this is set to 5%
of the image size.
3. Removing perspective distortions: The remaining candidates should be ana­
lyzed. To do this, the region of interest (ROI) of the original image is extracted based
on the corners of the found candidates. As the marker is square, the perspective
distortion can be removed by making the extracted ROI square. The resulting image
snippet is then thresholded using Otsu’s method.
4. Bit Extraction: The thresholded image snippet is now divided into a number of
cells, based on the size of the ArUco marker. E.g. here the size is 4x4 and with
border 6x6. In each cell, the number of black and white pixels is counted, and the
bit is set depending on which holds the majority.
5. Marker identification: Now the extracted bits can be used to identify the marker ID
by comparing it with a dictionary. Some correction of bit errors may occur, but the
30
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:40==
level can be adjusted to avoid false positives.
6. Corner refinement: Lastly, when the marker has been identified the estimated
positions of the marker corner points are improved. This is done similarly to the
checkerboard of the camera calibration, by using the OpenCV function
cv2.cornerSubPix(...).
Based on the procedure above, one may consider whether or not undistorting the image
before finding the AruCo markers is needed, as the marker detection is very robust al­
ready.
Still, the marker detection will become more robust by undistorting the images, as the
barrel distortion might inflict issues with the polygonal approximation. It may also give
better results for the visual servoing algorithm, as the barrel distortion will make the cor­
ner points move in a nonlinear way. If the image processing time becomes a bottleneck
of the system, one may consider skipping the undistortion.
4.5.2
Applying ArUco Marker Detection
The chosen ArUco marker design has been a 4x4 code (6x6 with border), and an initial
total size of 50x50 mm (with border). The size has later been changed to 80x80 mm
(without border) to improve visual servoing accuracy. This information is given to the
detection function.
The result of applying ArUco maker detection as described above can be seen in figure
4.7. Figure 4.7 (b) shows that the ArUco marker is detected (corners found) and the ID
is correctly read as marker 1. On (a) one can see that the same is possible for even very
blurry images, which may occur when the robot is moving. Here the marker is correctly
read as 0. Other markers and varying distances have also been tested.
Using larger markers has shown to be harder, and sometimes impossible, to detect with
the default threshold settings of the ArUco detection functions, as these are designed for
VGA resolution images [36]. Making a script (see appendix E.3) for testing the threshold
settings for a set of images covering the distance variations, has made it possible to find,
test, and verify new settings:
adaptiveThreshWinSizeMin = 251 (default 3)
adaptiveThreshWinSizeMax = 455 (default 23)
adaptiveThreshWinSizeStep = 100 (default 10)
Here the minimum window size is large, which may make it difficult to detect smaller
markers and markers that are far away. The window step size has been designed to
only take three steps, as using multiple steps (windows) will take more time and hence
reduce performance [30]. An example of two threshold test images can be seen in figure
4.8. (a). This show that the default values at maximum window size is not able to find
the marker. (b) show that after calibration the marker can be found instantaneous at the
smallest window size.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
31
==PAGE:41==
(a) ID 0, blurry.
(b) ID 1.
Figure 4.7: ArUco marker detection performed on a 4x4, 50mm ArUco code. Both images
are cropped.
(a) Using default parameters, at maximal win­
dow size the marker is not found.
(b) Using calibrated values, at minimum win­

5
Design of Digital Twin
As presented in section 2.4 the obvious choice for simulation was Gazebo which works
integrated with ROS. This chapter will describe how the simulator, the simulation environ­
ment, and the control have been set up to form a digital twin. This digital twin allows for
realistic testing before deployment on the actual system.
5.1
Simulation Environment
The ROS­Industrial GitHub package called Universal Robots [37] contains the UR5e robot
model and can be used with ROS and Gazebo for simulation. This only includes the stan­
dard robot with no equipment, and hence equipment has to be added.
Some of the objects to be simulated can be assumed static, while others can not and have
important physical properties. The physical properties of the robot and the equipment are
important, especially when simulating the force/torque application. To get somewhat re­
alistic measurements from the simulated sensor, one should model the movable objects
physical properties such as moments of inertia and mass.
5.1.1
Adding Models
Multiple models for the equipment and the stations are to be added to the simulated world.
The models can be added in two ways, either using URDF files or using SDF files.
From these files, one can describe a model’s appearance and physical properties using
shapes, colors, inertias, frictions, etc. The two file formats are very similar XML­based
formats, with only minor differences.
URDF files are mainly meant for describing robots, robot parts, and parts with sensors.
Therefore the UR5e, the FT300, the UR5e Adapter, the Fork, the camera, the button, etc.
are all created using URDF files.
SDF files are meant for other objects in Gazebo. These are the stations (DLP and CMM),
Mount Part A and B, and the remaining parts of the Print Base.
All the added models can be seen listed in table 5.1.
Table 5.1: Gazebo Models URDF and SDF models. *From Universal Robots Package
URDF
