Using Geometric Shapes and CAD Files
Both URDF and SDF file formats allow for some different ways of applying the shapes
of the models. While some models are made from simple geometric shapes, others are
imported from the CAD model files. The geometric shapes like cylinders and boxes can
be specified directly from the URDF or SDF, where one link is created for each shape.
Hence if the model is complex with many small parts, the complexity and size of the URDF
files quickly increase and become unmanageable.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
33
==PAGE:43==
An alternative is hence to use the CAD files directly. This can be done by converting the
SolidWorks drawings into COLLADA files (.dae). Solidworks does not provide such con­
version, but an STL file can be exported instead. This STL file can then be converted into
a COLLADA file using online tools, (here anyconv.com have been used). This does unfor­
tunately return a wrongly scaled file (as millimeters are interpreted as meters). Therefore
a simple MATLAB script have been created to automatically fix the scaling (see appendix
F.1).
When the shapes are determined, one can make more realistic impressions by applying
colors or textures.
URDF models
All the URDF models (except the button) are combined into the robot with equipment. The
table, the camera mount, and the camera have been created using geometric shapes,
while the other objects come from the CAD files. One model file (urdf.xacro) has been
created for each model part. By importing these files, and joining the models described
by them, one combined robot is created. An overview of the files used for the combined
robot model can be gained from figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: The list of files imported into stl_ur5e.urdf.xacro to model the entire robot with
equipment.
Camera Sensor: The camera sensor uses a sensor plugin for Gazebo called
libgazebo_ros_camera.so. Here it is possible to set parameters like the distortion coeffi­
cients, noise, resolution, etc. to make it mimic the real camera. The images are posted
to a topic called image_raw.
Force Torque sensor: The force torque sensor uses a Gazebo plugin called
libgazebo_ros_ft_sensor.so. Here it is possible to set the update rate as well as the noise
profile. The data is then posted to a topic called /ft_sensor/raw. The sensor must be
located in a joint, hence between two links. Additionally the joint must be revolute, but by
setting the rotation limits to 0° this can be considered a fixed joint [38]. Here it is located
between the tool and the FT300 model.
Button: As mentioned earlier an electromagnet keeps the Print Base locked onto the DLP
printer. To detach the Print Base this magnet must be disabled. This is done automatically
when the Fork activates the two buttons installed on Mount Part A.
Hence to mimic this behavior, a button has also been designed for the simulator. This
uses the Gazebo plugin libgazebo_ros_bumper.so, which senses the contact between
links, and publishes the data into a topic. By reading this topic it is possible to use a
package called gazebo_ros_link_attacher to detach the Print Base from the DLP. More
about this package can be read in section 5.1.3.
34
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:44==
SDF models
Several SDF models have also been created. The file format specification can be found
at [39]. These can be described by geometrical shapes or by using CAD files, similar to
the URDF models.
Here only Mount Part B has been created using the CAD files. A simpler model of Mount
Part A was created with geometric shapes, as the COLLADA model did not behave well
when interacting with the Fork.
Models of the stations have also been created.
ArUco Markers & Checkerboard: The ArUco markers have been designed from two flat
squares. The first contains the image of the marker, and the second is the background.
The marker image can be applied as a texture onto the front box. Similarly have been
done, to create a checkerboard for camera calibration tests.
5.1.2
Physical Properties
The model files can also contain information about physical parameters such as mass,
inertia, friction, etc. This is not relevant for all models. For example does Mount Part A
not need any collision or weight, as it is only included for visual purposes. Other models
like the camera, camera mount, FT300, and the adapter does not need to have collisions,
although they may have. Other models again may not need mass and inertia as they are
static like the stations.
An overview of the physical properties of each simulation object can be found in table
5.2. In short, all objects that should collide must have collision. Collision is optional if a
collision may happen but is not expected. Friction may be set accordingly. All objects
that are to be moved must not be static but must have specified inertia and mass. Some
objects, like the DLP printer, must not be static, as static objects cannot be attached and
detached (see section 5.1.3).
Table 5.2: Physical properties of Gazebo models. y: yes, is needed. n: no, must not
have, o: optional. *Uses default setup from the source package.

The main purpose of the simulator is to verify that the control and control algorithms, that
are to be developed, behave correctly. Still, it contains many uncertainties and will be­
have differently from the real­world robot. This is, as the control of the simulated robot
cannot be verified to exactly mimic the behavior of the real robot. Hence it is pointless to
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
35
==PAGE:45==
do over­excessive modeling. Instead, the controllers that are to be developed must be
robust enough to handle these uncertainties and final calibration may be performed on
the real system.
Still modeling the actual physical behavior to some degree is important.
While collision and static are binary on/off properties and will be set according to the
table, the remaining properties are not as simple.
Friction: In the physics engine of Gazebo (ODE), friction is modeled as a pyramid (ap­
proximation of a cone), and has numerous coefficients to adjust. Here only the coulomb
friction coefficients (mu and mu2) will be used. These describe the size of the two vec­
tors that create a plane orthogonal to the vector of contact, and they may have a value
between 0 and Infinity, resulting in frictionless contact, and contact that never slips, re­
spectively [40].
Mass: The mass of the objects is together with the inertia very important for the modeling,
especially when using the force/torque sensor. The mass is given in kg and is assumed
to be 1 if not specified. Therefore it is important to specify the mass. In this case, the
mass has been measured using scales, and the result can be seen in table 5.3.
Inertia Tensors: One can also specify the inertia tensors (3x3 matrix) for the parts. It
is possible to use SolidWorks (for CAD files) or MeshLab (for COLLADA files), to calcu­
late the inertia tensors [41]. This approach has fine results for the FT300 and the UR5e
Adapter but has shown instability with the Fork. Instead, the calculations have been done
manually, assuming simpler geometric models. Both approaches assume solid models
with evenly distributed mass.
The general inertia tensor can be described as:

5.1.3
Detaching the Print Base
To simulate the behavior of the electromagnet, a package called gazebo_ros_link_attacher
has been used. This package makes it possible to attach links of models to each other.
The general process can be described from figure 5.2. It requires that the models have
been spawned into Gazebo to work, and they must not be static. Spawning objects can
be done with the spawn_sdf_model service, which also works for URDF files.
There is a time delay from the object is spawned, till the object can be attached. There­
fore one must pause the Physics of Gazebo, to prevent the Print Base from falling before
it is properly attached to the DLP. Pausing and unpausing physics can be done with the
/gazebo/(un)pause_physics service.
Now by using the button contact topic, one can detach the Print Base from the DLP, when
the Fork collides with the button.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
37
==PAGE:47==
Figure 5.2: Attaching and Detaching the Print Base from python script using various ser­
vices.
5.2
ROS­Gazebo UR5e Arm Controller Setup
Accurately controlling the simulated arm is of great importance. To do this, especially two
control features are needed. This is the end­effector position and twist control. The con­
trol of the real UR5e does already provide such controllers via the Universal Robots ROS
Driver [18]. Hence the goal of the simulation controllers is to sufficiently closely mimic the
behavior of the real arm controllers.
The Position controller ensures control of the end­effector position and orientation (pose).
It is to be used whenever twist control is not needed. Examples of these situations are
initial station positioning and search (before ArUco markers are found), but may also be
used in other translation tasks, e.g. when moving the Print Base around. This is not to be
confused with a joint position controller, which controls the angular position of a joint.
The Twist controller (or velocity controller) controls the linear and angular velocities
(twist) of the end­effector. This is not to be confused with the joint velocity controllers
of the individual joints, which each ensures an angular joint velocity. The twist controller
is intended for use in the control loops of the visual servoing, and the force/torque control.
5.2.1
Controller Options
Initially, it was thought possible to use MoveIt! as the controller for the simulated robot.
This has shown not to be possible. MoveIt! provides a very nice framework for position
and trajectory­based control, where one can specify goal positions, waypoints, velocity
options, and accelerations options. Providing this information MoveIt! can calculate a path
or trajectory, avoiding obstacles, etc. This said MoveIt! does not provide the possibility of
velocity control, which is an essential part of the control [43]. Hence other solutions have
to be investigated. It is worth mentioning, that an extension to MoveIt!, called MoveIt!
Servo (previously known as JogArm), has later been found to exist. This should be able
to provide the wanted velocity/twist controller [43]. This possible solution was found after
the implementation of another candidate and was hence not pursued.
There exist some other options for controlling the velocity, but the implementation of these
are more comprehensive. In these solutions, one can only control each joint position or
velocity (joint state) individually, and as a consequence, one must also do the calculations
of the kinematics and velocity kinematics. The kinematic solutions have also shown very
useful for transformations between frames. Such transformations could also partially be
performed using the ROS tf package.
The joints can be controlled via Gazebo plugins or by using ROS Control. These options
will be covered in the following:
Gazebo Controller Plugin
An option for individual control of joint positions and velocities is to use Gazebo plugins.
Position Control: Can be done using a robot plugin. Here a Gazebo plugin listens to a
ROS topic to read joint state commands. These may then be used by the plugin to control
the joint angular position accordingly [44].
Velocity Control: Similarly one creates a plugin to command the joint velocity. But for
velocity this can be done using 3 different methods [45]:
38
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:48==
1. Set Instantaneous Velocity: This is an easy and well­supported approach, but one
loses the force applied by acceleration, which is not desirable for the force/torque
control.
2. Configure a joint motor (ODE only): Here forces from acceleration are included.
Using this, the velocity can be reached in a single update, making it somewhat an
ideal motor, for better or worse, as this is not a realistic behavior.
3. Create a PID controller: This is also well supported, and forces and accelerations
are felt. A disadvantage is that the controllers must be tuned, but this also gives a
more realistic behavior compared to the joint motor.
Of the 3 methods, the PID controller seems most appropriate, for a realistic simulation, but
does also require an extra work burden by tuning the controllers. The PID controller is not
a unique solution for the Gazebo plugins, but can also be created using ROS controllers in
a more straightforward way compared to creating and compiling a Gazebo plugin. Hence
the ROS Controllers solution has been chosen instead.
ROS Controllers
To use these one need the ros_control package. This contains another package
ros_controller, which holds plugins for a variety of different controllers [46], of these the
most interesting are:
1. joint_state_controller: Simply publishes all joint states of a robot.
2. position_controllers: Simply forward one or multiple joint position commands to
the joint. From this one could create a PID loop around it, based on feedback from
the joint states. This uses the PositionJointInterface hardware interface.
3. velocity_controllers: Receives one or more reference velocities and sends veloc­
ity commands using an internal PID controller. This uses the VelocityJointInterface
hardware interface.
4. effort_controllers: Has multiple sub­controllers for position and velocity. Here an
input reference (velocity or position) is given, and an effort output is calculated based
on an internal PID loop. These use the EffortJointInterface hardware interface.
The joint state controller publishes the joint states (joint position and velocity) continu­
ously but does not actually control the joints. As it is not desired to create an external PID
loop for the joint position controller, the effort controllers are used instead. Additionally,
to simplify the task and only use one hardware interface, the joint velocity controllers can
be set up as an effort controller as well.
Hence the effort_controllers have been chosen here. How to set up and launch the con­
trollers can be seen in appendices F.3 and F.4.1.
5.3
Tuning the ROS­Gazebo Controllers
Having established ROS communication between the robot joints in Gazebo and the con­
trol scripts, it is possible to tune the controllers of the individual joints to achieve a good
performance.
The controllers have been tuned twice. First tuning was without load and non­correct
inertias, as these had not been calculated yet. This first tuning allowed for verification
of the kinematics, which will be shown later, and therefore also verification of the control
approach in general in Gazebo.
The second tuning was done with load, to improve the simulated system performance un­
der stress, and with new and more correct estimates of inertia and masses as presented
in section 5.1.2.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
39
==PAGE:49==
Each controller is a PID controller, similar to what is shown in figure 5.3. The general
tuning process for these controllers has been a hand­tuning, based on the simulation
data and plots of the step responses. Although the arm joints are highly coupled, they
have been tested and tuned individually. This can be done, as the coupling from one
joint to the others can be considered a disturbance. Additionally, there is a significant
disturbance from the gravity, which usually is removed using a feed­forward term. This
is not an option for these controllers, and hence the controllers must be robust to this as
well.
Based on this, the following common procedure for PID tuning has been used:
1. Tune proportional gain kp
2. Tune integral gain ki
3. Tune differential gain kd, if needed.
In addition to this, the rules of table 5.4 may also be considered when tuning.
Table 5.4: How an increasing change in controller parameters will affect the system and
resulting signal. ”­” indicates small to no effect. *If kd is small. [47]
Rise Time
Overshoot
Settling Time
Steady­state Error
Stability
kp
Decrease
Increase
­
Decrease
Degrade
ki
Decrease
Increase

Degrade

­

Decrease
­

Acquiring data for tuning has been done mainly through the rqt_gui and by creating ROS
bags from the terminal. Exactly how this can be done, is described in appendix G.1.
Figure 5.3: A common PID controller for a joint, both angular velocity and angular position.
40
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:50==
5.3.1
The Joint Position Controllers
For the joint position controller, the following superficial requirements will be used as a
guideline for the tuning:
1. Zero steady­state error
2. Very small overshoot
3. Relatively long rise time (slower joint movements)
The most important requirement is the zero steady­state error, as it assures precise po­
sitioning of the arm. Secondly, a very small overshoot is desirable, as this will minimize
shaking, even when moving due to big step­changes of reference. Lastly, a relatively long
rise time is also desirable, especially for a real robot. As a fast settling time in general
also mean large angular velocities, which in turn result in very fast movement of the arm.
This concern especially large steps in the position reference, but this will in general be
handled by creating a trajectory towards the goal, rather than commanding a step straight
to it.
The simulated position controller will mainly be used for initial positioning, and therefore
the main focus has been on the first requirement. The actual performance of the second
and the third requirement is not supposed to be optimal, but rather sufficient. Ensuring
the performance of these two would better happen through trajectory control, rather than
simple pose commands. Developing a trajectory control is not required for this project,
and hence outside the scope.
The First Joint Position Controller
The first setup, tuning and design of the joint position controllers have found inspiration
from [48], which presents how to implement a joint position controller for a UR5 robot arm
in Gazebo. This has been a good foundation for the setup of a position controller for the
UR5e model instead.
Although the UR5 and the UR5e model are similar, they are not identical, which can also
be seen from the DH parameters of the robot models [49]. Additionally, the tools have
been added to the model as well (FT300, UR5e Adapter, Fork, etc.). Hence only some,
but not all, of the presented PID gains [48] could be reused. The initial untuned controller
gains and test plots can be found in appendix G.2.2. Additionally a description and tuning
of the first joint position controller can be found in appendix G.2.1 and G.2.3.
The Second Joint Position Controller, with Load
To tune the controller for the worst­case scenario, the load had to be added. Addition­
ally, some of the models had been updated to have more accurate physical properties in
accordance with table 5.3. This changed the system enough to make the first controller
unusable. Therefore the controller had to be re­tuned and become more aggressive to
account for the extra load. Again the task becomes more difficult, as the disturbance
of the gravity also increases. Therefore the tuning had to start over from scratch. Us­
ing the rqt_gui, live­tuning can be performed, by observing the controller response while
changing the controller parameters. With this approach, the gains of table 5.5 were found.
Table 5.5: PID gains of the final joint position controllers.
Shoulder Pan
Shoulder Lift
Elbow
Wrist 1
Wrist 2
Wrist 3
kp
1000
500
500
30
100
30
ki
0
750
500
25

25
kd
10

10

3
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
41
==PAGE:51==
The kp gains have been tuned to avoid to large overshoots. Large overshoot may happen
in the direction of gravity, while undershoots may happen when working against gravity.
Additionally, the higher load has made the gravitational impact larger. It is hence not pos­
sible to remove both completely, but instead to find a kp that satisfies a happy medium.
In general, compared to the first controller, the differential gain kd has been decreased,
while the integral gain ki has been increased significantly. The integral part must now
work harder (be more aggressive) to reach zero steady­state error. Still, decreasing this
will give very long settling times, while increasing it would result in a larger overshoot.
kd has then been tuned to minimize the overshoot, but would, if chosen too big, increase
the settling time and result in undershoot.
The step responses of the controller became as can be seen in figures 5.4 and 5.5. The
step sizes are π/2 rad and ­π/2 rad, with an exception for the shoulder lift as it would col­
lide with the ground for higher step values than 0.8 rad. The settling time is in general
less than 4 seconds. This is an increase compared to the less than 1 second settling
time of the first controllers. As there were almost no overshoots for the first controller, the
overshoots here are also in general significantly higher. The Base joint is good and does
not have any overshoot, while the shoulder lift and elbow joint experience maximal over­
shoots of about 20%. For the wrist joints, a maximum overshoot of about 10% is seen.
The overshoots highly depend on the position of the arm and hence the gravity impact.
Fortunately, all the controllers do settle nicely with no static error. It is worth pointing out
the plot for the Shoulder Lift Joint. As it crosses to 1.57 rad (vertical upright position) it
gets an overshoot, that seems to settle for some time (about 8 seconds), before recov­
ering. The exact reason for this has not been discovered, but the command value does
also seem to settle at 0, which can be seen in figure G.10, in appendix G.2.4.
In the same appendix one can see the plots for the same controller tested without load
(figure G.7, G.8, G.9). In general these show the same behavior. As there is no load, the
controllers are a little more aggressive, resulting in a little larger overshoots but also a bit
faster settling time.
As all the controllers eventually do settle correctly with no steady­state error, the con­
trollers are asses to work sufficiently well for the intended simulation purposes of crude
initial positioning.

5.3.2
The Joint Velocity Controllers
The requirements for the joint velocity controller are a bit different from the joint position
controller:
1. Zero steady­state error
2. Small overshoot
3. Very fast settling time
As the joint velocity controllers are to be used together with the inverse velocity kinemat­
ics (IVK), to ensure a precise twist of the end­effector, the velocities must be as precise
as possible at any given moment. This is as the IVK commands assume that all joint
velocities act ideal (hence are equal to the setpoints/references).
Therefore the controllers must be both fast and precise, with as little overshoot as possi­
ble, as any deviation from the given reference will result in an error of the final twist of the
end­effector.
The First Joint Velocity Controller
An initial P­controller was chosen, in accordance with the hand­tuning approach described
above. The gains and the resulting performance of this initial controller can be seen in
appendix G.2.5. Tuning from this, the PID gains of table G.4 in the appendix was found.
Here the differential gain has not been set in any of the controllers, hence making them
PI controllers. This is, as it was not found necessary to include the differential part to
improve the controller performance.
The plots of the step response for each joint can be found in appendix G.2.6, figure G.14,
G.15, G.16. All the step responses are relatively fast, with settling times less than 0.5
seconds, even for larger step changes of 1 rad/s. At the same time, all controllers have
only a very small or zero steady­state error, as can also be seen on the error plot (figure
G.16). The last two joints have oscillatory behavior because they are the last joints in a
coupled chain. Hence small oscillations from simulation and control errors will cascade
and increase toward these joints.
The Second Joint Velocity Controller
As for the joint position controller, the first joint velocity controller was not robust enough
to account for the extra load. Therefore it had to be re­tuned. Initially, live­tuning was
tried, using step responses similar to those presented for the first controller. This gave
seemingly good results, but when tested on a circular movement (see section 5.5.3) the
controllers were not fast enough to follow the trajectory, resulting in an ellipsoidal trajec­
tory.
By performing live tuning based on the circular movement instead, it was possible to
achieve much better results. From this, the PI(D)­gains of table 5.6 were found.
Table 5.6: PID gains of the second joint velocity controllers.

The resulting step response plots, for a test with load, can be seen in figure 5.6, 5.7, and
in figure G.20 in appendix G.2.7. Here steps are tested for changes in the velocity of 0.2
rad/s and ­0.4 rad/s. As can be seen, the controllers are quite aggressive and gives a
relatively large overshoot. But this overshoot is also quickly settled (within 0.1 seconds),
and will hence not result in large displacements. For all the controllers the settling time
is less than 0.1 seconds. Finally, the steady­state error is zero. One can also see the
oscillations in the last wrist joints, similar to the first controller. Still, the joint do in general
follow the velocity set, and this is hence not seen as an issue.
Similarly can be performed for the case with no load, the result of this can be seen in
appendix G.2.7 on figures G.17, G.18, and G.19. Here the overshoot is a little larger and
the oscillations small, but it is not significant.
To see the circular trajectory test, the reader is referred to section 5.5.3.
As the controllers follow the velocity references sufficiently accurately and fast, they are
assessed to work well for the intended simulation purpose of following twist commands/­
trajectories.

5.4
UR5e Robot Kinematics
With the controllers tuned and working, it is time to consider the kinematic modeling of
the robot. This will make it possible to calculate the forward kinematics (FK) and then the
inverse kinematics (IK). The IK will allow for position control. It is worth noting that the IK
was calculated and found before the actual tuning of the joint velocity controller. This made
it possible to lock the joints, hence making testing much easier. After calculating the FK,
it is also possible to find the velocity kinematics (VK) also known as the Jacobian. Using
the inverse velocity kinematics (IVK) allows for twist control. The IVK is also sometimes
referred to as the inverse Jacobian. The following sections will go through the calculations
and the testing of these in the simulator.

5.4.1
Forward Kinematics
The robot forward kinematics (FK) describes the geometric relationship between the end­
effector pose and the joint angles of the robot arm. This makes it possible to calculate
the position and orientation of the end­effector from the given joint angles. This pose is
given in relation to the robot base (in robot base frame coordinates), and can hence be
considered a transformation from the base frame to the end­effector frame [4].
As mentioned earlier, the UR5e is a 6DOF robot with 6 revolute joints and a stationary
base. Each joint is connected by a rigid link and has a different orientation/revolutionary
axis.
The way to find the transformation from the base frame to the end­effector frame is by
dividing the robot arm into multiple frames. In fact one frame for each joint (see figure
5.8). This way it is possible to describe the position of a joint frame, based on the previous
joints, due to their geometrically rigid relation. Using a number of these transformations in
a chain results in one complete transformation from a given start joint to a given end joint.
Using all the transformations, from the base frame to the end­effector frame will yield the
forward kinematics of the robot [4].
To get the actual transform, one will have to formulate the above mathematically.
A robot with n joints has n + 1 links, as each joint connects two links. When the ith joint
is actuated, the ith link (and any subsequent joints and links) will move. We can attach
a coordinate frame Oi = (xi, yi, zi)T to each of the joints. Each of these frame positions
must be based solely on the previous joint frame (Oi−1). This way, when the ith joint
move, the position of the subsequent coordinate frame (Oi+1) will change in relation only
to the ith’s frame (Oi). The subsequent (ith +2) joint frame’s positions to the ith +1 frame
will remain constant. This way the joint frame positions have been decoupled.
As the ith and ith −1 frame is related geometrically, one can describe a homogeneous
transformation (denoted Gi) from one frame to the other. This transformation will then be
46
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:56==

5.4.2
Inverse Kinematics
The inverse kinematics (IK) does, as the name suggests, the opposite of the forward kine­
matics. In IK one supplies the wanted end­effector pose and from this, the equivalent joint
angles are calculated. Here it is worth noting that multiple joint configurations may be so­
lutions to such a calculation. One may especially imagine a left­armed and a right­armed
solution, as well as solution variations including elbow­up and elbow­down. Hence often
4 different solutions exist.
To do these calculations the FK is needed. Taking the approach presented by Ryan Keat­
ing for a UR5 [50], the same calculations can be done for the UR5e. Again this takes a
purely geometrically analysis to calculate the resulting joint angles using the DH param­
eters of table 5.7. Only the results will be included in the following, for a more in­depth
study the reader is referred to [50] which holds good explanations and additional support­
ive figures.

5.4.3
Velocity Kinematics
The velocity kinematics (VK) revolves around describing the linear and angular velocities
(twist) of the end­effector of the robot in relation to the angular velocities of the individual
joints. This means that the twist of the end­effector can be calculated from the angular
velocities of the joints. As the forward kinematics describes the relationship between the
joint positions and the end­effector, these can be used for calculating the velocity relation
as well. This is done by calculating the Jacobian of the forward kinematic equations [4].
In general, one can write the velocity kinematics as the following [4]:
ζ = J ˙Q,
with Q = [q0, q1, ...qn]T = [θ0, θ1, ...θn]T
(5.23)
Here ζ is a 6x1 vector comprised of the linear velocities V 0

joint variables, as mentioned earlier all joints are revolute, and hence ˙qi = ˙θi. Lastly, J
is the Jacobian matrix. This is comprised of two sub­matrices, JV and Jω, describing the
linear and angular velocity respectively. Each of these is of size 3 x n [4]:
ζ =

==PAGE:60==
There are singularities to the VK solution, but these will not be handled here. Instead, it is
assumed that the robot of the simulator will work in a well­defined range, where reaching
singular positions does not happen. Testing the calculations for the velocity kinematics
has been done through the inverse velocity kinematic solution, and can be seen in section
5.5.3.
5.4.4
Inverse Velocity Kinematics
With the velocity kinematics in place, one can calculate the inverse velocity kinematics
(IVK). This will make it possible to find the joint angular velocities that will satisfy a wanted
twist of the end­effector. This can simply be done by rewriting equation 5.23 [4]:
˙Q = J−1ζ
(5.28)
Calculating the inverse requires the matrix to be square and non­singular. Fortunately, as
the arm has 6 joints, the Jacobian is a 6x6 matrix. This said the matrix does get singular
in certain configurations, and hence one may use the pseudoinverse of the Jacobian
instead. But again for the simulator, it can be assumed that the robot will work in well­
defined configurations.
Testing the IVK calculations will be covered in section 5.5.3.
5.5
Results: Testing the Kinematics
The kinematic calculations have been used to create some python libraries. These aid
in the calculation of transformations via the FK, and in finding the IK and IVK solutions
needed for the position and twist controller respectively. The UR5e FK library is in ap­
pendix I.1.3 and the IVK is in appendix I.1.4. These are tested in the following sections.
5.5.1
Testing the Inverse Kinematics
The FK calculations have been tested and verified by commanding joint positions to the
robot, then reading the coordinates of the end­effector using Gazebo. The same joint po­
sitions, as was commanded, could be plugged into the calculations yielding the theoretical
position of end­effector. These two sets of coordinates matched for multiple different joint
configurations.
It is worth noting that the above FK calculations do not take the offset of the robot into
account (it is put on a table).
5.5.2

different wanted end­effector poses. The tested poses can be seen in table 5.8, and all
have the same end­effector orientation. The actual poses of the UR5e in Gazebo can be
seen in figure 5.9. The pose P1 corresponds to a 0 rad reference for all joint angles.

Where d is the diameter of the circle and τrot is the rotation time. For this test d = 0.2m,

Looking at figure 5.15, the joint velocity controllers follow the reference calculated for the
simulator (Gazebo Ref.) so well that it mostly can’t be seen. By doing a calculation in
MATLAB based on the data, a theoretical reference can also be created for comparison.
Two such references have been made, Theo. Ref. uses the current actual joint positions,
while the Pure Theo. Ref. only has been initiated with the same start values as the sim­
ulator. As can be seen, both nicely follow the actual simulated data.
This said there is a deviation between the simulator and the purely theoretical result, which
also affect the actual position of the end­effector. This can be seen in both figures 5.16
and 5.17. Here the end­effector position ends up describing a spiral rather than a circle,
as the coordinate curves get larger. This is purely the consequence of the simulator refer­
ence update time. The end­effector will move along the tangent last described by the twist
command until a new command is calculated. If this is not done frequently enough, the
position will deviate. In the presented plots, a new reference for the theoretical solution
(Pure Theo. Ref.) is only calculated for every 4th time sample, which makes the approx­
imation closer to the simulation. Actually, the period between the IK simulator updates is
a little larger, and hence the result starts to deviate more as time goes by.
Looking at figure 5.16, one can see that one period of the curve takes about 7 seconds
(from 13 to 20), this is well in line with the wanted speed τrot = 6s, as the spiral movement
makes the trajectory longer. From this it can also be seen, that the initial radius seems
very close to 0.1 m, in line with the diameter d = 0.2m.

5.5.4
Optimizing and Testing with New Joint Controllers
The calculation and behavior of the kinematics have been verified with the previous. But
as mentioned earlier, new joint controllers had to be designed to account for the increased
load when moving the Print Base. Hence it has been interesting to shortly verify that the
general control using IK and IVK still works. Additionally, small changes for optimizing
the calculations of the IVK have been performed. This includes hard coding values of
constants in the transformation matrices and changing the approach of polling data from
the joint_state topic to instead using the latest values. Combined do the improvements
result in a decrease in average calculation time from 0.0893 seconds to 0.0225 seconds,
making it possible to run with about 44 Hz compared to the previous 11 Hz.
The result of a new combined test can be seen in figure 5.18, where the system is under
the +2kg load. The first part is a position test of the FK. Here it moves from P1 →P2
→P3 →P0. All points are reached with no significant error in position. In P0 it follows
a straight line trajectory along the x­axis, which can be seen at the red line of (a). Then
it moves back to P0. From here it makes a circular movement. As one may notice, the
circle is less like a spiral than before. This is due to the increased calculation frequency.
Looking at the period of the coordinates it is 6.5 seconds (from 78.04 to 84.54), closer to
the desired 6 seconds than it was before, but still, it moves in a spiral. The signal plots
are not included here but follow the reference similar to what is seen in the above tests of
IK and IVK.

5.6
Using the URSim and the UR5e
Although the Gazebo simulator is very well suited for simulating the system behavior when
implementing force/torque control and visual servoing, it does not guarantee realistic be­
havior of the robot arm. In this regard, it is better to use the simulator from Universal
Robots, named URSim. It includes the e­Series arms and has been designed to very
closely mimic both the control and the behavior of the arms [51]. It can be installed both
on a virtual machine or a separate computer. From here it will, communication­wise, act
exactly like the real robot, as one has to connect to it over an Ethernet connection, and
as it uses the same driver for control and communication. This means that the robot and
the simulator can switch places with no serious change in the control approach.
The graphical interface of the URSim is identical to that of the UR Teach Pendant (the
control tablet for the UR robot arm). The only difference is, that the robot displayed inside
this, is not based on the position of a real robot, but simply the simulated robot instead.
Hence with URSim, it is possible to verify the behavior of the twist and position control,
before deploying on the real system, with almost no extra work.
Notes on how to install the URSim can be seen in appendix H.1. URSim and the UR5e
are set up identical for remote control, notes on this can be seen in appendix H.2.
To control the real robot and the URSim robot, a ROS driver has to be installed. The
driver for the e­Series robots is named Universal_Robots_ROS_Driver, and can be found
at [18]. Here a guide for installation can also be found. Notes on how to setup the driver
and the recommended real­time kernel can also be found in appendix H.3
After setting up the URSim/UR5e and the driver on the ROS­Master PC, network com­
munication has to be established. Again notes on how to set up this communication can
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
57
==PAGE:67==
be found in appendix H.4. In this appendix, some notes are also given, on how to run the
driver.
With all of this set up, one can now control the robot, using the twist and trajectory pose
controller provided by the driver. To ease the use, a python library (URDriverControl.py)
has been created and this can be found in appendix I.1.5.
5.7
System Setup
Combining the parts of the system previously described results in a system setup as
shown in the diagram of figure 5.19. It illustrates that the system of this project may run
in 3 different ways:
1. Simulated on one Linux machine. Here only the Gazebo simulator is used on the
ROS host machine.
2. Simulated on two Linux machines. One machine runs the ROS host (ROS master)
and another runs the URSim. These are connected with an Ethernet connection as
described in appendix H.4.
3. On the real system. This is similar to the URSim but also includes feedback from
the camera and/or the force/torque sensor.
As can be seen, there is no feedback from the URSim solution, as it does not provide sen­
sor information. This ultimately means that it can only be used for verification of movement
commands. This has been used in the initial phases to verify the behavior of the Gazebo
control commands, before running these on the UR5e. Initially, it was thought to use the
FT300 via USB/RS485 connection, this has been abandoned in favor of the internal F/T
sensor. Therefore the feedback of the real system is provided through the camera USB
connection and Ethernet connection. What is similar for all 3 solutions is, that the joint
states of the robot can be read through the ROS topic /joint_states/. Only slightly differ­
ent approaches are needed when going from the Gazebo simulation to the real system,
as libraries with commands for controlling twist and position have been developed (see
appendix I).
Figure 5.19: The system setup. This show how the different parts and subsystems are
interconnected to form the combined system.
58
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:68==
6
Visual Servoing
Visual servoing is the chosen solution for positioning the robot end­effector. Here an eye­
in­hand configuration will be used, where the camera is mounted onto the end­effector.
Additionally, an image feature­based visual servoing (IBVS) approach has been selected.
The following sections will describe the necessary theory for the implementation of the
visual servoing algorithm, based on these choices.
6.1
The Image Jacobian
The image Jacobian (or Interaction Matrix) L can describe the twist ζ of the camera frame,
based on the velocity ˙S of a set of image features S in the image plane. The relation is
linear and can be written as [4]:
˙S = L(S)ζ
(6.1)
This can be utilized similarly to the calculation of the IVK. By using the inverse of the image
Jacobian one can describe the velocity of the image features, based on the velocity of the
camera. This can later be used to design a control law, that minimizes the error between
observed features and reference features (i.e. the features observed when the robot is in
the desired position).
Different approaches to calculating the image Jacobian exist, and they also depend on
the type of features chosen [4]. Here the chosen image features are the normalized
coordinates of the ArUco marker corners in the camera frame. The image plane corner
coordinates (u,v), measured in pixels, can be found with OpenCV as described in section
4.5. These must be transformed into normal coordinates (ˆx, ˆy) of the image plane Π with
center in OI as shown in figure 4.4. This can be done with the following calculation [12],
based on the perspective projection:
ˆx = x
z = u −u0
fkf
ˆy = y
z = v −u0
f
(6.2)
Here the coordinates u, v, u0, and v0 may be measured directly in pixels if the focal length
f also is. kf is a scaling factor of the pixel size, but is 1 here, as the pixels are square
[27]. It is worth noting, that this perspective projection does not take the distortions into
account, and hence the calculations must be performed on the undistorted image.
For such a set of image features, one can now describe the image Jacobian. This is
done by finding an expression for the derivative of ˆx and ˆy, based on the relation to the
perspective projection and the velocity of the camera frame ζ [12] [4]. These expressions
can be written into a matrix form as follows [12]:
[ ˙ˆx
˙ˆy
]
=
[−1/z
0
ˆx/z
ˆxˆy
−(1 + ˆx2)
ˆy
0
−1/z
ˆy/z
1 + ˆy2
−ˆxˆy
−ˆx
]
ζ =
∆Lζ
(6.3)
From here on the dependency of L on S will be omitted for brevity. The image Jacobian
here depends on the image feature (ˆx,ˆy), as well as the distance z to the feature. In many
cases, z can be difficult to obtain, but due to the well­known dimensions of the ArUco
marker, the distance can be estimated using the perspective projections. One can see
that the angular velocities do not depend on the depth z, and hence errors in estimating
the distance will only affect the linear velocities, which can be compensated by the con­
troller [4].
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
59
==PAGE:69==
6.1.1
Finding Corner Positions
The easiest way to determine the positions of the corners is to first find the pose of the
marker, and then calculate the corner positions from this. Finding the marker pose can
be done with aruco.estimatePoseSingleMarkers(...). This can return the rotation and
translation vector of the marker. The rotation vector Rmarker can be converted to a ro­
tation matrix Rmarker using cv2.Rodrigues(Rmarker). Based on this, one can find the
translation of the corners from the center:
Rmarker = [Rx, Ry, Rz],
Xcorner = w
2 Rx,
Ycorner = w
2 Ry
(6.4)
Where w is the marker width. One can now find the pose of the individual corners using
the translation vector Omarker:
Pcorner = [x, y, z]T
PUpper Left = Omarker −Xcorner + Ycorner,
PUpper Right = Omarker + Xcorner + Ycorner
PLower Right = Omarker + Xcorner −Ycorner,
PLower Left = Omarker −Xcorner −Ycorner
(6.5)
Hence the z value of the corner feature can be extracted from this. It is worth noting, that
newer versions of this OpenCV function can directly provide the corner poses, but this is
not an option for Python 2.7.
6.1.2
The Inverse Image Jacobian
A single point is not enough to account for all the 6DOF of the robot. With only one point
one can find motions that would not make observable changes to the image feature(s)
[4]. E.g. the image feature of a single point does not change, when moving along the
projection lines. To avoid this issue at least 3 points (6 features) are needed, but more
can be used [4]. One can expand the image Jacobian for more point features by simply
stacking the above matrix of equation 6.3. For n point features this will result in:
S = [ˆx1, ˆy1, ˆx2, ...ˆyn]T
−→
L = [L1, L2...Ln]T
(6.6)
In this case, all 4 corner points can be used. Hence there are more feature parameters
than task DOF, and therefore some of the features are redundant. Typically this results in
inconsistent equations as the measurements are prone to noise and quantization errors.
Therefore a single solution cannot satisfy the equations completely. The solution is to use
the least squares solution [4] where the pseudoinverse of the image Jacobian may be
found with the following equation [5] [4]:
L+ = (LT L)−1LT
(6.7)
Using this, it is now possible to calculate the twist of the camera frame, based on desired
velocities of the image features:
ζ = L+ ˙S
(6.8)
6.2
IBVS Control Law
The goal configuration of the visual servoing is defined by a set of goal image features
Sgoal. The error function is hence given as:
E(t) = S(t) −Sgoal
(6.9)
With this and equation 6.8, it is possible to set up a proportional control law given by [5]:
ζ = −λL+E(t)
(6.10)
60
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:70==
Here λ is a constant gain matrix with values > 0. The error E gives the velocity of the fea­
tures needed to eliminate the error, and L+ translates this to a camera velocity. Therefore
one can adjust the rate, with which the error is eliminated by tuning λ accordingly [11].
E.g. λ = 0.1 · I will give a 10% error decrease per second (as the velocity is in m/s). This
can also be seen from the error dynamics of the system, where ideally the pseudoinverse
Jacobian L+ should cancel out the true behavior Ltrue when closing the loop [12]:
˙E = −λLtrueL+E
(6.11)
Hence when LtrueL+ = I, one get the desired error dynamics:
˙E = −λE
(6.12)
6.3
Twist Transform
The previous sections describe how to determine the image Jacobian, and with this how to
describe a control law. The output of this control law is a desired twist (ζc
c) of the camera,
which will decrease the error of the image features.
This twist is given in the camera coordinate frame c, and can hence not be used to control
the end­effector directly. Instead, it must be transformed to the robot base coordinate
frame 0.
A twist in one frame can be transformed to another frame using an adjoint representation
of a transformation [23]. This means, that by knowing the transformation from the end­
effector frame to the camera frame, one can calculate the camera twist in the end­effector
frame (ζ6
6) [4]:
ζ6
6 =
[R
Skew(O)R
0
R
]
ζc
c,
Skew(O) =


0
−Oz
Oy
Oz
0
−Ox
−Oy
Ox
0


(6.13)
Skew(O) is a skew­symmetric matrix based on the translation vector O. The rotation

6.4
Gamma Mapping the Twist
As the image features get closer to the goal features, the velocity decrease. At some
point, the velocity will be so small that the last adjusting movement will take a very long
time, or worse, the arm stops moving too early. An approach well­known from image
processing has been chosen to avoid this. This is namely gamma mapping. Here an
input value can get a nonlinear gain depending on the mapping [52]. Within control, this
is also known as gain scheduling. In practice, this makes it possible to put a higher gain
on smaller values, and a more neutral gain on higher values.
Before applying this, the velocities have been limited to the range of [­vmax,vmax] (here
with vmax = 0.05 m/s or rad/s), to avoid large velocities.
The gamma mapping function can be described as follows:
vtemp = |vin/vmax|γ
vout = vtemp · sign(vin) · vmax
(6.16)
As can be seen, the actual gamma mapping is performed on the unsigned scaled values.
After the mapping, the values are re­signed and re­scaled. The result is a function that
can be described by figure 6.2, where the gain is higher at low velocities and neutral at
higher velocities.
Figure 6.1: Coordinate frames of the end
effector and the camera. As can be seen,
the camera frame has both a rotation and
a translation in both y and z.
-0.05
0
0.05
vin
-0.05
0
0.05
vout
Gamma Mapping  = 0.6
Figure 6.2: Gamma Mapping for γ = 0.6.
There is an increased gain for smaller val­
ues and a neutral gain for larger values.
The mapping has been scaled to the max­

6.5
Combined Visual Servoing Design
A combined diagram of the visual servoing controller can be seen in figure 6.3. It sum­
maries the results of the previous sections. First, the marker is detected, yielding the
current image coordinates (u,v) of the corners. Using the perspective projections gives
the image features (S). The image Jacobian L, and hence the pseudoinverse image
Jacobian L+ can be found from these image features together with the distance to the
marker z. With the Jacobian and the image feature errors, a camera twist ζc
c can be found.
This may be transformed back into world frame, and scaled using gamma mapping to give
the twist reference ζref for the velocity controller.
62
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:72==
Figure 6.3: Block diagram of the implemented visual servoing. The superscripts indicate
the related frame (6 for end­effector frame, 0 for base/world frame).
6.6
Limitations of IBVS
It is worth noting, that IBVS may suffer from some control problems. The proposed IBVS
controls the camera motion based on an image error. This means, that there is no explicit
control of the trajectory of the camera. Ultimately, this may result in motions exceeding
the capabilities of the manipulator [4]. The velocities of the manipulator have been limited
as described above, to cope with this. Additionally, cases for rotations of the features
around the camera optical axis can pose problems as well [4]. This should not give large
issues here, as the position and orientation of the markers are fixed, and therefore the
initial orientation of the end­effector can be placed appropriately. Alternatives include par­
titioned approaches [4], but this has not been deemed necessary for this application.
The control algorithm may reach local minimums instead of the desired pose. These can
be hard to predict [12], and therefore a range of different initial poses will be tested in the
following sections. These poses are designed to cover more than the expected uncer­
tainty of the actual crude initial positioning.
The IBVS is a process­heavy task, where large images are to be analyzed multiple times
per second. It is desirable to get a fairly high sample frequency, as it allows for higher ve­
locities and helps prevent overshoots and oscillations, hence giving faster settling times.
The image size, undistortion, thresholding parameters, and whether or not to display
and/or save the images are main factors in the speed of the program. Both displaying,
saving and, using full­size images have resulted in a frequency of about 7 Hz, which has
worked sufficiently. Using a smaller image size is not desirable as it gives a lower reso­
lution of the error estimate. By not saving and displaying the images one can reach 8.5
Hz. Additionally, by not undistorting the image the frequency can reach about 15 Hz. One
might turn on these knobs if deemed necessary.
6.7
Results: The Visual Servoing in Gazebo
A visual servoing algorithm has been developed based on the theory of the previous sec­
tions, and implemented into a python library (IBVS.py) which can be found in appendix
I.1.2. This has been tested in the Gazebo Simulator and the test code can be found in
appendix I.2.1. The test setup is as can be seen in figure 6.4 (a). As can be seen, the
DLP printer is located along the negative y­axis. Therefore a reference/goal image of the
ArUco marker has been taken in the pose: Pgoal = [­0.002, ­0.444, 0.658, ­π/2, π/2, 0.0],

This test is then comprised of moving the robot arm to other arbitrary initial poses, and
letting the IBVS control steer the robot arm until a view of the ArUco corners is similar to
those of the reference image. A total of 7 poses have been tested, and these can be seen
in table 6.1 (with k=1). All the poses are retracted 18 cm from the goal and are otherwise
offset differently. In general, these poses more than cover the variation of the expected
initial position. Crone and Lønkjær have defined a requirement for the precision of the
visual servoing for the detachable build plate base system. This is ±1 mm in x, y, and z;
and ±0.017 rad in roll, pitch, and yaw [3]. These requirements will also be the baseline
here.

though the feature errors would become close to zero, the actual pose error would become

average 14 seconds.

6.8
Results: The Visual Servoing in Real Settings
A visual servoing algorithm similar to that used in the simulator has been implemented
for the real system. The test code can be found in appendix I.2.2. Again it is important
to test a variety of different initial positions, to cover the expected variation of the crude
initial positioning. A goal pose was found to be: Pgoal = [0, ­0.53, 0.6, ­π/2, π/2, 0]. Using
the same definitions for the initial poses as can be seen in table 6.1 (with k=1), the 7 tests
have been carried out on the real system. During the tuning, it was found unnecessary
to perform the gamma mapping of the velocities. Instead, λ was increased from 0.1I
to 0.15I. Again the maximum velocity has been set to 0.05 (m/s or rad/s) due to safety
reasons. The test setup can be seen in figure 6.6.
Hence for this test γ = 1, λ = 0.15 and vmax = 0.05 have been used.
The results are summarized in table 6.3. One can see that the errors no longer are zero,
but still, they are all well within the requirements. This of course also applies for the
mean and the standard deviation of these test errors. The feature errors are smaller than
5.768·10−4, which is equivalent to the error of 1 pixel. Again a set of example plots for the
Random 1 initial pose, can also be found in figure 6.7. Although it is difficult to see from

would scale the smaller velocities such that the end­effector would not become too slow
too early.
Initially, it was attempted to implement the gamma mapping as well, but this gave unde­
sired oscillations at the start of the trajectories. Due to time constraints, the first solution
was accepted, and fixing the gamma mapping was remembered as a future improvement
possibility. Fortunately, there was time for improvements which can be seen in section
6.8.2 below.

6.8.1
Extracting the Print Base
Before the Print Base can be removed, one must know when the system is within the re­
quirements. By analyzing the plots for each of the tests, it has been possible to estimate
such a threshold for the image feature error. The requirements are met when the feature
error is smaller than 0.001. Using the perspective projection equations, one can find this
equivalent to no more than 1 pixel of error for each of the features (as 2 pixels = 0.0012
feature error).
Using this thresholding value, it is now possible to know when the goal is reached. The
next step is to move the end­effector straight forward, along the end­effector z­axis. This
can be done by setting a velocity of e.g. 2cm/s in this direction and then doing a twist
transformation similar to the one presented in section 6.3 to get it in the base frame.
With the given goal image, the velocity command time could be tuned to 9.5 seconds.
This would push the buttons and release the Print Base. After the first calibration, this
was tested for each of the 7 initial poses, some multiple times, and all with success.
Videos of the visual servoing tests from pose Random 1 and 2 can be found at:
https://youtu.be/6I-DhV50-NA, https://youtu.be/8_HnvPijIK0 respectively.
6.8.2
Improving the Real IBVS
Although fast time performance is not required, it is still desired.
As the image sample frequency is relatively low, one cannot accept too high velocities.
This is as a high velocity could make overshoots that are corrected too slowly due to the
low sample frequency. Combined this can result in oscillatory behavior. This oscillation
was especially an issue at the beginning of the trajectory, where the ˆx and ˆy image errors
are relatively large. These errors result in the (world) y and z velocities that can be seen
in figure 6.8 (c). The problem would become worse as the gain was increased. Therefore
gamma mapping would not be a solution for this issue, as this would increase the gain on
these smaller velocities relatively more.
Instead, the solution has been to use a larger gain and to utilize the velocity range (±vmax)
more. In practice, this has been done by increasing λ, and by scaling the velocities equally
whenever a velocity of the twist ζ was larger than vmax.
This can be formulated as:
vout =
{
vin
if max(|ζ|) < vmax
vin
vmax
max(|ζ|)
if max(|ζ|) > vmax
(6.17)
This gave a far better separation between the velocities produced by the x and y image
errors, and the velocities produced by the distance (z) error. This made it possible to
increase the λ to 0.3I.
Hence the parameters of this test became: λ = 0.3I, vmax = 0.05. A goal position was
found to be: Pgoal = [­0.1656, 0.5530, 0.5892, 1.922, 1.535, 0.350]. Again using the same
definition for the initial poses as stated in table 6.1, the new controller could be tested.
The result of this can be seen in table 6.4. Here the IBVS stops when the threshold cri­
terion is reached, and that can also be seen from the feature data where multiple points
show 1 pixel of error (5.5 · 10−4) and the rest show 0 pixel error. One can see, that at
these points the pose requirements are also met, with no absolute error larger than 0.8
mm and 0.002 rad. After these poses were reached, the end­effector was able to move
forward through the guides of Mount Part B and hence prove that extraction was possible.
Because this test does not allow the visual servoing to continue beyond the threshold, its
precision cannot be directly compared to the previous tests. Still, it shows a smooth tra­
jectory (see figure 6.8 (b)) and compliance with the requirements.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
69
==PAGE:79==
The main purpose of this test implementation is to improve the settling time of the visual
servoing. The settling times of the old and the new implementation can be seen in table
6.5. Here one can see that the goal is reached about 2.5 to 3.4 times faster with the
improved implementation. In average it is 3 times faster, which is significant. Where the
settling times before were more than 50 seconds, they are now no more than 20 seconds
(e.g. see figure 6.8 (a)).
The improvement is mainly caused by the increased velocities in small error regions, fa­
cilitated by the increased gain. As mentioned one could fear that this gain would lead
to oscillations, but as the velocities are scaled as shown in equation 6.17 this does not
become a problem.
Videos of the improved visual servoing tests from pose Center and Random 1
can be found at: https://youtu.be/JI0bHuaIXOY, https://youtu.be/pb-diEhZYzY re­
spectively.

the errors are calculated at the sample that satisfies the threshold value of 0.001 feature
error. *is times 10−4.

7
Force Torque Control
As mentioned earlier, the chosen force/torque control strategy is admittance control. This
reacts to interaction forces by making changes to or deviating from, the desired motion.
Special types of admittance control exist, e.g. compliance control, where the closed­loop
system can be characterized as a mass­spring­damper with specified mass, stiffness, and
damping. If one omits the stiffness, the resulting control scheme is known as damping
control.
The Universal Robots ROS Driver does not contain a controller for pure position control,
only for control of position trajectories. On the other hand, it does contain both a twist
controller and a controller for the individual joint velocities [18]. Therefore the motion will
be controlled through the end­effector velocities.
The use of a F/T sensor makes it possible to achieve linear and decoupled behavior. This
means that it is unnecessary to model the robot dynamics, as the dynamics resulting from
the velocity controller can be decoupled from the force/torque control design [12].
7.1
Admittance Control Approaches
Although there is a clear baseline of how admittance control should work, there are many
different approaches to the actual implementation.
For example, a UR5 has been adapted to make ultrasonic scans in [53]. Here admittance
control prevents large forces from being excerpted onto the patient. The actual implemen­
tation presents a mapping, using a nonlinear gain matrix, between the measured forces
and torques, and the output velocity.
Similarly, in another instance for a surface tracking system [54], a proportional gain matrix
is also used to calculate the pose deviation, which again can be used as reference for a
position controller.
In [55], a damping control approach is presented for a peg­in­hole task. Here a calibration
method is presented together with the actual control law. For the controller, one can spec­
ify the desired inertia and damping of the system, and as input, one can specify desired
velocities and forces/torques. The result is a velocity reference that can be commanded
to the robot velocity controllers.
None of the above­presented approaches use the position error as feedback. Hence they
cannot be classified as full compliance controllers, but are instead more similar to damp­
ing controllers. This is because the pose error could be used to calculate compensating
spring forces using the stiffness term. Instead, the controllers are settling with any posi­
tion, that satisfies the desired forces and velocities.
Whether or not this is sufficient will be discussed in the following. First, it is worth men­
tioning that methods for compliant controller designs (with pose error compensation), can
be found in [12].
7.2
Task Requirements
To place the Print Base onto the CMM, the following steps should be taken:
1. Initial positioning using visual servoing.
2. (Optional) Additional positioning, using position commands.
3. The manipulator should lower the Print Base until the Print Base is in contact with
the surface.
72
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:82==
4. The manipulator should keep lowering the Fork until it is touching the bottom of the
Print Base guides.
5. The manipulator should retract until free of the Print Base.
For the steps 3­5, force/torque control is needed to adjust/limit the forces. The Print Base
is lowered onto a well­known flat surface, and there should be no disturbances apart from
this. Hence force control in the vertical direction is the only requirement. When retracting
a small pressure onto the Print Base should be kept. Otherwise small position error could
make the pegs on the fork attach to the Print Base and drag it off again.
7.3
Force/Torque Sensors
Before the F/T controller can be implemented, the F/T sensor has to be chosen. For this
project 3 known F/T sensors have been available. These are as follows:
1. Internal F/T sensor of the UR5e: 500 Hz sample frequency, force range of ±50 N,
force precision of 3.5 N [56].
2. FT300­S from Robotiqe robotiq: 100 Hz sample frequency, force range of ±300 N,
force precision of 0.1 N [57]
3. FTN­mini40 SI­40­2 from Schunk: >560Hz sample frequency, force range of ±120
N, force resolution of 0.02 N [58]
All of these sensors can deliver a measuring range of at least 50 N, which is sufficient to
sense more than the 2 kg of load (Print Base). The resolution and precision of the sensors
vary a lot, with the internal sensor being the worst candidate.
Each of the sensors had pros and cons in relation to their availability and the project time
schedule. The internal F/T sensor was of course available as part of the robot, and con­
venient as the measurements can be read of a ROS topic as part of the UR5e driver
interface. Concerns about this sensor were raised mainly due to the speed of the sensor
and its precision being relatively low. The speed concern came from Universal Robots’
ROS Support, who mentioned there being a delay from the force is sensed, to the robot
reacts [59]. Therefore the internal F/T sensor was initially disregarded.
The FT300­S was considered the main option of this project, as it had and still was being
used for other projects with success. This is Despite a relatively low sample frequency of
100 Hz. It is designed for the UR5e end­effector, and hence easy to mount, though it still
requires external software for data collection. As this sensor was in use for other projects,
it could only be used for a limited time and planned ahead.
Optionally the last option (Schunk FTN­mini40) was presented, as it was more widely
available. This sensor would require the design of a suitable mount for the UR5e, as it
does not natively fit onto the end­effector. This would also require external software for
control.
It was not found feasible to design a new mount and integrate the software needed for
the Schunk sensor, due to the project time limits. Instead, the FT300­S was expected to
be used.
As the FT300­S was chosen early in the project, many of the tests and considerations,
including simulation, visual servoing, and scene analysis, have considered this part.
The design of the F/T control went ahead of schedule, hence the FT300­S sensor was
not available when the first F/T control demo was ready for testing. This gave rise to
test the performance of the internal F/T sensor instead. This went above expectations,
and delivered a very reasonable result, with much better precision than expected from the
specification sheet. Therefore it has not been deemed necessary to try the implementation
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
73
==PAGE:83==
with the FT300­S.
7.4
Damping Control
Stiffness behavior is not required as the end­effector should only move down and settle at
a specified sensed force in the vertical direction. Therefore one can use damping control
as described earlier.
The implementation presented in this section is based on [55]. First, the transformation
from the sensor to the end­effector will be considered. Next is the calibration of bias
forces, and lastly the actual control law.
7.4.1
Wrench Transform
The chosen control approach assumes controlling the forces and velocities in the world/base
frame. This requires a transformation of the F/T sensor data from the sensor frame s to
the base frame 0.
The F/T data is comprised of 6 values, 3 linear forces, and 3 torques (rotational forces).
This is also known as a wrench. Hence this task is similar to the twist transformation of
the camera frame presented in section 6.3, as twists and wrenches are similar quantities.
Hence we can transform the F/T values of the sensor frame F s
s into the end­effector frame:
F 6
6 =
[R
Skew(O)R
0
R
]
F s
s ,
T 6
s =
[R
O
0
1
]
(7.1)
Again with Skew(O) being the skew­symmetric matrix as specified in equation 6.13. The
rotation matrix R and the translation matrix O can be found from the transformation T 6
s
between the sensor frame s and the end­effector frame 6. Here we have two cases:
The first and easiest case is the internal F/T sensor, where the sensor frame s and end­
effector frame 6 coincide. Hence R is an identity matrix, and O = 0.
The second case is for the FT300­S sensor. This is used in the Gazebo simulation. Here
the sensor does not coincide but is shifted along z (here assumed to be 1 cm). Addition­
ally the sensor frame is rotated equivalently to Roll = π rad, Pitch = −π/2 rad, and Yaw =
0 rad.
When the sensor data have been transformed into the end­effector frame, it can be trans­
formed into the world/base frame using the forward kinematic transformation matrix T 0
6
(see section 5.4.1), using the current joint angles. This gives [4]:

7.4.2
Removing Bias
For the controller to work properly, bias forces and torques should be removed. Here it
will simply be done by taking the mean of a large set of raw F/T samples. These samples
will be taken with load while the end­effector is held still in mid­air.
Fbias =
∑i=n
i=1 Fi
n
(7.3)
[55] presents a more advanced method compared to this. Here force samples are taken
in different end­effector poses which allows for a more precise determination of the sensor
bias and gravity effect of the tool and load.
Such an advanced method is not necessary here, due to the simple nature of the vertical
task. Additionally performing such calibration may show difficult or even impossible, as
the load is not rigidly attached to the tool.
74
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:84==
7.4.3
The Damping Control Law
The admittance controller chosen for this project can be described by the following control
law [55]:
Fd −F = Md( ˙ζd −˙ζ) + Bd(ζd −ζ)
(7.4)
Here Fd is the reference (desired) wrench and F is the measured wrench after trans­
formation to the world frame. Md is the desired inertia matrix of the system and Bd is
the desired damping matrix of the system. Both are diagonal positive definite matrices,
meaning that the force and torque controllers all are decoupled and hence independent.
Lastly ζd and ζ are the desired and actual twist of the end­effector.
This means that the system has two control inputs, one for a desired twist, and one for a
desired wrench.
This controller is a damping controller as there is no stiffness specified. The stiffness con­
tribution would, like for a spring­mass­damper, be dependent on the positional displace­
ment, but as can be seen, this system only depends on the velocity and acceleration.
By rewriting equation 7.4 and performing a Laplace transformation one get:
ζd −ζ =
1
Mds + Bd
(Fd −F)
(7.5)
With s being the Laplace operator variable. (ζd −ζ) is the control signal (twist reference)
for the twist controller, and (Fd −F) is the error signal.
A diagram of the controller can be seen in figure 7.1. Here the index i refers to the
ith diagonal element of Md and Bd, related to the ith element of the twist and wrench
vectors. The superscripts indicate the related frame, e.g. 6 for end­effector frame, and 0
for base/world frame.
Figure 7.1: Block diagram of the implemented damping controller. The index i refer to the
ith diagonal element of Md and Bd, related to the ith element of the twist and wrench vec­
tors. The superscripts indicate the related frame (6 for end­effector frame, 0 for base/world
frame).
7.4.4
Controller Discretization
The controller presented above is a continuous time controller. In order to implement this
in a control program, one has to discretize it. This is done by performing a z­transformation
based on a Tustin approximation, accordingly replacing s in equation 7.5 with [60]:
s = 2(z −1)
ts(z + 1)
(7.6)
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
75
==PAGE:85==
With z being the z­domain operator, and ts the sample time (1/frequency). The choice of
sample frequency will be covered in later sections.
7.5
Controller Implementation
This section will cover the implementation and testing of the damping controller described
previously in section 7.4.
7.5.1
Implementation
The controller has been implemented in a Python script, to ease the integration with the
rest of the system. In contrast to the visual servoing, the damping controller requires a
fixed real­time sample frequency to work. Although the python timer and callback capa­
bilities have been utilized, this is not an optimal solution. This is because the script runs
in soft real­time, and hence there are no guarantees on the actual execution frequency.
Although tests have shown correct mean execution frequencies, it covers relatively large
variation at times (see section 7.5.2). Despite this, tests have not shown to be significantly
affected by it, and due to project time limits, the python solution has been accepted for
now.
As mentioned the discretized controller is implemented in a timer callback function, en­
suring relatively accurate sample frequencies.
The first tests in the Gazebo simulator used the same standard values for Md and Bd as
[55]. These values did show unstable behavior, and therefore the inertia matrix and the
damping matrix were increased by a factor of 10 resulting in:
Md = diag([10, 10, 10, 1, 1, 1]) and Bd = diag([1600, 1600, 1600, 60, 60, 60]).
These parameters did give stable behavior. As all models in the simulator are rigid bod­
ies, the forces very quickly increase, and hence result in more noise than would actually
be the case. Therefore the testing of the force/torque controller early moved onto the real
system for further tuning.
As the contact point of the Fork is offset compared to the force/torque sensor, a torque
will be sensed around the end­effector y­axis (see figure 6.1). The controller will try to
remove such a torque by moving away from the source, hence doing an undesired ro­
tation in the torque direction. Therefore the control of this torque has been disabled by
simply disregarding the raw sensor values (setting them to 0). Another undesired torque
comes around the end­effector x­axis, which when invoked may also result in undesired
movement. Hence this has also been disabled. The last torque has been kept, as it has
nice properties when lowering down onto surfaces that have a tilt around the end­effector
z­axis.
7.5.2
Selecting Sample Frequency
Based on the above­stated controller parameters, it was possible to test the effect of the
sample frequency. This was performed on the real system.
A selection of frequencies from 50 to 200 Hz was tested, with the Fork moving onto a rigid
flat surface. The desired reference force was set to 10 N. Using the standard deviation of
the force σF when in contact with the surface (see table 7.1), one can see that the sample
frequency does not have a big influence. The standard deviation of the sample times σts
increases slightly with the frequency. At the same time, σts has a larger influence the
higher the frequency is. With 1 σts deviation this means that the 50 Hz frequency may lay
between 47 and 52 Hz, while the 200 Hz may lay between 150 and 298 Hz, although the
standard deviations are close to the same.
Therefore the higher frequencies are in general unsuited for control. At the same time,
50 Hz is a rather low frequency for control, and therefore 100 Hz has been chosen as
the happy medium. It must again be stressed, that a varying frequency is far from ideal
76
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:86==
for real­time control. Despite this, it has fortunately shown to work quite well with good
stability, even in the 200 Hz test cases.

at steady­state.

7.6
Results: The Damping Controller in Gazebo
As mentioned a Gazebo test of the controller was created, and the test code can be
found in appendix I.2.3. This uses the above­described parameters for Md and Bd, a
100 Hz sample frequency, and has the torque control around the end­effector y­ and x­
axis disabled.
The test setup can be seen in figure 7.2, where the robot is to move the Fork down onto
the white box. The result of the test can be seen in figure 7.3. This shows a significant
amount of spiky oscillations on the measurements, that originate from the contact between
the rigid bodies. Some of this noise can be removed, by taking an average of 10 samples,
which have been done and shown with darker colors. Here one can see, that the z­axis
value gets quite close to the reference of 10 N indicated by the black line.
There are some outages in the force, which e.g. can be seen at 16 s, here the white box
in Gazebo for some unknown reason got squeezed briefly. This has been a general issue
for the tests in Gazebo when making forces between two non­static objects. Additionally,
the Gazebo twist controller is not perfect and has some difficulty ensuring zero motion.
Therefore the end­effector ends up rotating slowly around the y­axis, as was not desired.
This may also be the reason why the force never reaches the 10 N reference. Fortunately,
this is not the case for the real robot controllers.
