7.7
Results: The Damping Controller in Real Settings
The initial tests of the damping controller in the real settings were performed with a refer­
ence of zeros forces. This made it possible to test the correct behavior of the controller
compliance, by simply pushing the robot arm around. As the controller will work towards
zero forces, the arm could be pushed around (similar to the freedrive mode on the teach
pedant).
When the behavior was verified to work correctly, the next step was to test it out. Here
the first test was a force reference of 10 N in the (world) z­direction, making it push down
onto a surface. Initially, a soft foam rubber block was used to protect the equipment. After
verification, the controller was tested on a rigid surface, as is the case for dismounting the
Print Base (see figure 7.5 (b)). The test code can be found in appendix I.2.4.
7.7.1
Tuning of the Parameters
With a decided sample frequency of 100 Hz, the inertia matrix and the damping matrix
could be tuned for better responses. As the previously stated parameters gave a rather
fine result, in terms of variation around the desired reference, these laid the foundation
for the tuning.
An increase in the inertia (mass) matrix would result in larger force variations, but no
change to the constant velocities. Hence resulting in a more aggressive controller when
in contact, because it contributes based on the derivative (i.e. changes) of the forces. An
increase in damping would decrease the velocities and hence also the force variation, as
its contribution is inverse proportional to the sensed forces. Still, it is not desirable for the
force/torque controller to move too slowly, and hence a trade­off must be made.
Through hand­tuning, the inertia matrix was chosen to be Md = diag([1, 1, 1, 0.1, 0.1, 0.1])
and Bd = diag([1600, 1600, 1600, 60, 60, 60]). This is a smaller inertia matrix, making it less
aggressive when in contact. The damping matrix is kept the same, as the end­effector
would otherwise move too slowly.
In figure 7.4, a test before (first row) and after (second row) calibration is shown. Here the
Fork is to push against a flat rigid surface, similar to the Gazebo test case. Therefore the
references for all F/T are set to 0 N, except for the force in z (vertical) direction, which is
set to 10N. Similarly, all velocity references are 0. One can see that the uncalibrated case
works very well and that the calibration only contributes to dampening the noise around
the reference. There is only a small noticeable difference, as the standard deviation for the
first case is 0.31 compared to 0.27 for the calibrated case. For both cases, the maximum
deviation from the reference is 1.1N, but it is in general much smaller as can be seen from
the standard deviations. The rise and settling times are identical for the two, at approx. 2
seconds. This of course depends on how rigid the Fork and environment are. The choice
of damping matrix could also affect this but does not, as it is identical for both tests.
In general, both tests do show a very satisfying and stable behavior of the force/torque
control, reaching the reference with only small oscillatory deviations.
Figure 7.5 (a) shows a force/torque control test using an oblique surface. Here the torque
control can be seen to effectively adjust the orientation of the end­effector to remove the
sensed torque. Doing such orientation adjustments for the other axis is not desirable, as
previously mentioned in section 7.5.1.
Figure 7.5 (b) show an example of the used test setup. Here the surface (white board) is
tilted for an oblique surface test, but may also lay flat for a flat surface test.
Videos of the force/torque control tests for the flat and oblique surface can be found
at: https://youtu.be/VF3dgbetkzk, https://youtu.be/ggO3RwCwfIU respectively.

7.7.2
Testing Dismounting of Print Base
The previous tests only considered the Fork pushing against some surface. The case is
a bit different when the Print Base is mounted.
Here a calibration is performed to remove the negative (downward) bias of the Print Base.
Setting a small desired force in the upward (positive) z­direction will hence still make the
end­effector move down. When the end­effector moves down, the weight of the Print
Base is released. Hence the value added to remove the bias will be sensed.
This means, that when using only small positive force references, the end­effector will stop
moving before the Fork hits the bottom of the guide. Consequently making dismounting
impossible.
There are numerous different solutions to this. One could be to calibrate before and after
the load (Print Base) is inserted. Hereby switching the calibration when the weight of the
load is released.
Another and bit easier solution is to use the knowledge, that the Print Base has an almost
constant weight of about 2 kg ( 20 N, equal to the load bias). Instead of using the small
desired force in the upward (positive) z­direction, one could simply add these 20 N. In
other words, add the bias to the reference. This way, when the load is lifted, the added
bias to the reference will cancel the bias of the lost load.
The last solution of the two has been used here due to its easy implementation, although
it is less general than the first.
The last step of dismounting is now to simply move backward while keeping the pressure
onto the surface. This can simply be done by setting ζd to move in the negative z­direction
of the end­effector frame (e.g. using twist transformation to the world frame).
When moving backward, the Fork will at some point no longer be in contact with the Print
Base. When this happens, the end­effector will be moving downwards, trying to create the
desired force. Here it is important to either keep the desired force small (hereby keeping
velocities small), or to know the exact distance/time of the release and then switch to
position or velocity control. This has been tested and shown to work very well.
Two tests of the dismounting can be seen in figure 7.6, the upper row is for the normal
flat surface, and the lower row is for the oblique case. Here the Print Base is without build
plate (hence approx. 400g = 4N). Again a reference of 10 N is used to get a relatively
fast and firm contact with the surface. Hence it moves until the 10N is sensed. After 30
seconds, a new reference of 5N is set, this is enough to cancel out the bias of 4N plus a
little extra to secure the surface contact. At the same time, the end­effector starts to retract
by setting a positive velocity reference on the x­axis (0.01 m/s). At about 41 seconds, the
Fork is released from the Print Base. As no forces actually affect the end­effector, only
the bias of the Print Base is now visible (approx 4N). This only gives a slow downward
motion while the end­effector continues to retract.
From all the dismounting tests, the Print Base has not shown to move during retraction,
hence standing still where it was put.
A video of the force/torque control dismounting tests for a flat surface (see figure
7.8 (a)) can be found at: https://youtu.be/uOeaTJolcLQ.
80
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:90==
Figure 7.6: Force/torque control dismounting test plots. The first row shows the normal
surface case. The second row, shows the oblique surface case.
7.7.3
Testing on the CMM
A test similar to the previous dismounting test has been carried out on the CMM. Here the
full Print Base was used as can be seen in figure 7.8 (b). In figure 7.7 one can see the test
result. At 15.5 seconds the Print Base touches the CMM and the Fork creates a firm con­
tact with the bottom of the guide, using a reference of 25 N. At 20 seconds, the reference
is lowered to 19 N to just cancel the 17 N bias of the Print Base. At the same time, the
end­effector can retract with a movement in the y­direction. After 26 seconds the control
approach was switched to velocity control, as the pegs were free from the mounting hole.
This way the end­effector can move further backward and free of the Print Base without
lowering. Even though the CMM glass surface is smooth, the Print Base has not shown
to move during retraction here either.
A video of the force/torque control test on the CMM can be found at: https://youtu.
be/dozfOVEW35s.

Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:92==
8
Combined System Test
This chapter will cover the combination of the different presented control methods for a
combined task execution. This involved the velocity control, the position control, the visual
servoing, and the force/torque control. All are key parts in making a successful execution
of extraction, relocation, reinsertion, etc.
8.1
Extraction and Relocation
The initial goal of the project was to enable extraction and relocation of the Print Base.
This would showcase the functioning control methods for a real practical task. Such a
task can be divided into sub­tasks as can be seen below:
1. Initial uncertain positioning at the DLP printer
2. Visual servoing
3. Extraction
(a) Velocity control: Move forward
(b) Magnet release
(c) Velocity control: Move down + backwards
4. Move to a new initial uncertain position at a station
5. (Optional, if needed) Use visual servoing
6. Dismount the Print Base
(a) Force/torque calibration
(b) Force/torque control (move down)
(c) Force/torque control (keep down + move back)
This is a fairly simple task as the previous chapters describe how to complete each of
the subtasks. Hence the developed control will only have to be combined and tuned for
the setup. The most difficult part is to get a good reference image for the visual servoing.
This has to be very accurate due to the narrow tolerance, and therefore it can take a lot of
time and many tries to get it right. Fortunately, when the calibration works, it will continue
working. Hence taking a good reference image is worth the time in the long run.
A good reference image for the real DLP system can be seen in figure 8.1 (a), and addi­
tional images of the test in phase 3, 6b, and 6c can also be seen in (b), (c), (d) respectively.
A full video can be found at:
https://youtu.be/JyVrlFPy2ow
The video shows a successful execution test comprised of step 2, 3, 4, and 6. The exe­
cution time is fairly high at about 3 minutes for the steps. This is about 1 minute for the
visual servoing (step 2), 1 minute for extraction (step 3), 20 seconds for moving to the
station (step 4), and lastly 50 seconds for the force/torque control (step 6). The execution
time is high, as there was more focus on a safe execution rather than a fast and opti­
mized execution. Therefore the velocities have been set quite low for both step 3 and 4.
Additionally, one might perform a faster force/torque calibration, better initial positioning,
and faster retraction to decrease the execution time of step 6. This test was done before
the development of the improved visual servoing, but using this instead of the original, as
shown, would also greatly decrease this execution time.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing

==PAGE:93==

8.1.1
Human Interaction and Potential Automation
The video presented above shows the Print Base being put onto two wood­blocks, at a
waiting station, to protect the print. If the Print Base is to be put onto the CMM, it must be
put upside down such that the print is facing upwards. This requires the Print Base to be
taken off the Fork and re­inserted upside down (e.g. after cleaning etc.).
For now, this would be a human operation, and an example can be seen in the video
found at:
https://youtu.be/xHJ2tNrqoAU
The test code for this example can be found in appendix I.2.5. In this test a human operator
removes the Print Base from the Fork after it has been extracted. Then the print is cleaned
and re­inserted such that it can be put down. In the video, it is just put onto the wooden
blocks for simplification, but it might as well have been calibrated for a completely flat
84
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:94==
surface like the CMM.
If the print must not be cleaned (or similar), human interaction may not be needed if a
contraption at the CMM is developed. Such a contraption could be similar to the wooden
blocks, allowing the robot to put it down without harming the print, then dismounting,
rotating the 180°, remounting, and rotating back to its original orientation. Then the Print
Base would automatically have been put upside down. This will block most of the camera
view, which must be taken into account for further steps. One may either utilize the limited
view or trust the current position. This possibility is left for further possible testing and
development.
8.2
Additional Task: Reinsertion
One of the motivations for automation is to enable automatic continuous printing. To
realize this, a new Print Base must be inserted.
Both the videos linked above also present such a task after the extraction and relocation.
Here a new Print Base is available at a known location at the waiting station. Therefore
one can use simple position control to pick it up. After returning to the DLP printer, the
same goal image can be used for positioning. Then for reinsertion, one can do the inverse
steps of extraction: Moving down, forward, and then up. The upwards motion uses the
force/torque control to protect the printer. To dismount the Print Base from the Fork,
force/torque control is used similar to dismounting at the waiting station.
Now the printer can continue printing. The steps are summarized in the following list:
1. (After extraction and relocation)
2. Position control to the new Print Base
3. Extraction
(a) Velocity control: Move forward
(b) Velocity control: Move upward
4. Move to new initial uncertain position at DLP printer
5. Visual servoing
6. Reinsertion
(a) Velocity control: Down
(b) Velocity control: Forward
(c) Force/torque calibration
(d) Force/torque control (move upward)
7. Dismount the Print Base
(a) Force/torque control (move down)
(b) Force/torque control (keep down + move back)
Images of this execution in phase 3, 6b, 6d, and 7b can also be seen in figure 8.2 (a),
(b), (c), and (d) respectively. Again time optimizations may be performed similarly to the
suggestions mentioned in section 8.1.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing

==PAGE:96==
9
Discussion
This project has faced many different challenges and limitations, both practical and theo­
retical. Some of these have been overcome through the work of this project, while others
have been subject to temporary workarounds and simplifying assumptions. This chapter
will cover some of the challenges that have been encountered during this project, and
improvement suggestions for these as well.
9.1
The Work Flow
This project has covered many overlapping and entangled subjects, and for the same
reason project planning and execution have been very important. The project started by
getting an overview of the system, the problem statement, and how it could be solved
with the given remedies. This includes a general investigation of how the parts, visual
servoing, force/torque control, and communication worked. Based on this preliminary
phase a plan and a time schedule was developed (see appendix B).
The time schedule was an important tool and has been followed very well with only a
few deviations. The most significant deviation was an underestimation of how long time it
took to set up the simulator (joint controller tuning and re­tuning) and how long time was
needed to edit the report. The final combination and testing were done before completing
the documentation of the F/T control, and hence this had to be finished after the real DLP
test videos were taken.
As there was some time to spare, it was decided to do some improvements to the visual
serving, making it run faster.
Apart from this, the time schedule has been followed nicely.
9.2
The Digital Twin
The digital twin in Gazebo has shown to be a very useful tool, that worked quite well for
simulation of both the visual servoing and the force/torque control. Although it is not com­
pletely accurate in terms of behavior it is fairly close and more than sufficient to verify the
behavior of the control algorithms presented.
The largest issue with the digital twin has been the controller design. As expected, the
position controller cannot be compared with the actual position (trajectory) controller of
the real UR5e, because it simply steps to the joint angles that yield the desired position.
Still, as it has shown no steady­state error, it has worked very well for the intended task of
initial positioning. One might improve it by implementing algorithms for trajectory control.
The velocity controller has on the other hand worked significantly better. Although the con­
troller has an overshoot, it is fast and follows the set velocity references nicely, as needed
for simulating the visual servoing and force/torque controller. The largest drawback of the
controller is its incapability to ensure zero velocities. This has especially been an issue
for the force/torque control, where the end­effector drifts a little such that the orientation
changes when forces are applied to it. This is fortunately not the case of the real system,
and the drift is small enough to still verify the overall functionality of the force/torque control
algorithm. Unlike the position controller, there seems to be no easy fix for this issue. In­
stead one might want to the change control approach, and maybe try implementing other
existing control options, like the JogArm package. As mentioned previously this package
was not tested as the controllers already were set up when this was found. Having known
about this package from the project start might have saved a lot of time on the simulator
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
87
==PAGE:97==
implementation (assuming that it works). Fortunately, the time is not considered wasted,
as a sufficiently well­working solution is presented. Additionally, it gave valuable knowl­
edge and experience on the subjects of transformations and kinematic modeling.
9.3
The Visual Servoing
The chosen IBVS approach may have some undesired trajectory behavior, especially in
relation to rotations of the target. If the target has a large rotation compared to the goal,
the trajectory will retract the camera (and end­effector) away from the desired position
while rotating into the desired orientation [12]. This is not considered an issue, as the
initial position and orientation can be well controlled. Still, other approaches with more
ideal trajectory behavior exist. E.g. by combining the estimated image Jacobian with a
Jacobian estimated at the goal (where the distances to the image features (z) are known)
[12]. The estimation of the distance z does also include the estimation of the actual pose
of the marker and hence the image features. This also allows for using pose­based visual
servoing (PBVS) and even combining it with the IBVS for a hybrid or partitioned control.
This has not been explored due to the time constraints and scope of the project.
Lastly, the IBVS approach may suffer from local minimums [12], preventing the controller
from reaching the goal. Estimating these minimums are difficult, but as the controller has
been tested in a wide range of possible initial positions with success, these may not show
up as issues.
The first controller version tested on the digital twin had faster and better properties com­
pared to the one initially implemented on the real system. This partially has to do with
the camera system being more ideal, as distortions are not present. Therefore the digital
twin can more easily remove the error completely and settle. Additionally, the Gazebo
controller parameters seemed a little too aggressive on the real system. Therefore the
gamma mapping was initially skipped, and only the λ was tuned. When the controller
worked stably and well, it was accepted despite the slow settling time, as this was not a
requirement and as there remained a force/torque controller to be designed. This is also
why this controller was used for the combined tests. As there was time to spare, improve­
ments to the visual servoing were implemented, yielding significant improvements to the
execution time.
9.4
The Force/Torque Control
The internal force/torque sensor was used instead of the FT300­S, despite it seemingly
should have worse performance. Nonetheless, the observed performance of the internal
sensor has been way above expectations. Using the internal sensor has also made the
testing and development easier, as data collection, conversions, etc. have been done
through the robot driver.
The assumption of using the FT300­S has a significant influence on some of the analyses
made in this report. E.g. the scene analysis, and the visual servoing assumes that the
FT300­S prolongs the end­effector. This influences the FoV and precision respectively.
The choice of using the internal sensor was made after the visual servoing was devel­
oped, so removing the fake FT300­S part used in the tests may improve the precision.
The designed admittance (damping) controller has shown to work very well for the task
of putting the Print Base onto flat surfaces. It even has a very good force precision with
only quite small deviations. Additionally, it allows for concurrent velocity control that has
been very useful for detaching the Fork from the Print Base and even for remounting a
new Print Base onto the DLP. Although other force/torque control approaches exist, there
seem to be no good arguments for using these above the one presented in this project
88
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:98==
for the given task.
Some of the torques are disabled, as the offset between the sensor and the contact point
induces undesired behavior of the controller. It may be possible to model the forces af­
fecting the Fork based on the sensed values, but this is outside the scope of this project.
For the same reasons, it can only handle surfaces that are oblique around the end­effector
z­axis.
The implementation of the force/torque controller does not guarantee a constant sam­
ple frequency, which is a potential issue that should be fixed. This said it has actually
not given any problems during the design, calibration, or testing. Still, it is a theoretical
requirement of the controller that hence should be met. One might try to access the real­
time capabilities of the real­time kernel, or maybe distribute the F/T controller calculations
to an external real­time device such as a microcontroller or similar.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
89
==PAGE:99==
10
Conclusion
The aim of this project has been to automate the part of the soft tooling process involving
the extraction and relocation of 3D prints. This automation can facilitate a more stream­
lined and effective production, where fewer human actions are needed. Additionally, this
can also improve and promote a healthier working environment as human contact with
the used toxic liquid polymer is limited.
In that connection, this project has successfully presented a visual force/torque control
hybrid solution for a UR5e robot manipulator. This has allowed to demonstrate the most
important automation features, namely the extraction and relocation. The visual servoing
allows for a very high­accuracy initial positioning, which is demanded by the small tol­
erances for extraction. The force/torque control aid in limiting the forces of interaction.
Both are very important when working in an uncertain environment, as is the case of this
project.
The project has investigated and explored different possible solution approaches. Here
with the aim to find the most appropriate and well­suited solution for the given problem, en­
vironment, and available equipment. This has led to a study of the theoretical background
and existing literature on subjects like robotics, visual servoing, force/torque control, im­
age analysis, simulation, and control.
There have been created and modified equipment for the DLP 3D printer used in the soft
tooling process. Additionally, a digital twin of the soft tooling laboratory and robot manip­
ulator has been created in Gazebo. This has allowed for simulating the developed control
algorithms before deployment on the actual system.
This project presents a dynamic image­based visual servoing approach with an eye­in­
hand configuration. This has shown to work very well, delivering a pose precision within
the set requirements of ±1 mm (in x, y, z) and ±0.017 rad (in roll, pitch, yaw). The goal is
reached when the image feature error is below a certain threshold, and this has shown to
assure a precision with an error no larger than 0.5 mm, 0.003 rad. The execution time of
the first presented controller was in average about 54 seconds, for the tested initial poses.
This was later improved to about 18 seconds in average, by implementing some changes
to parameters and velocity scaling.
The presented force/torque controller is a damping (admittance) controller, as it does not
include a stiffness term. The internal force/torque sensor of the UR5e is used to cre­
ate feedback for the controller. It produces a velocity reference for the UR5e velocity
controller to follow. The force/torque controller only controls the end­effector x, y, and
z forces and the torque around the z­axis. This is to prevent undesired motions by the
remaining torques. The resulting controller has shown high accuracy for reaching the de­
sired forces. The precision is characterized by a standard deviation from the reference of
0.27 N. With the force/torque controller, it has been possible to put down the load (Print
Base) onto rigid flat surfaces (i.e the CMM) and dismount it from the end­effector while
controlling and limiting the forces to desired values.
A combined system test has been performed and presented, where it has shown possi­
ble to extract the Print Base from the real DLP printer, relocate it (possibly with human
interaction), and lastly also insert a new Print Base into the printer, hence allowing for
continuous printing.
In conclusion, the project has successfully presented a visual force/torque control solution
for the given setup.
90
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:100==
10.1
Suggestions for Future Improvements
Testing the system has shed light on some of the greatest challenges to the system. Cal­
ibrating the visual servoing by finding a good goal image has shown very difficult. This
is not made easier by the, in fact, very narrow tolerances of the design of the detach­
able build plate system. Many of the design tolerances are possibly smaller than what
is needed, and one could consider making the guides higher, the peg lower, and making
the fillet larger on the prong tips, etc. All of these would make it much easier to do the
extraction. Still, it is possible with the system as it is, and when a good goal image is
available the system does not seem to fail.
When detaching the Print Base, making the guides higher and lowering the peg would
also help.
Using the switches on Mount Part A to detach the Print Base has not shown to be a good
solution. Too often either of the switches is not thoroughly pressed, and hence no de­
tachment happens. Additionally, there is not far from pressing the button to pressing the
mount part (and hereby the printer). Pursuing other solutions for detaching the Print Base
is hence recommended. This could e.g. be done from the UR5e PLC when in detach
position.
One might strongly consider using the developed force/torque control more actively for
the detaching as well. This could be done by using the velocity references of the force/­
torque controller. This would make it possible to greatly confine the forces if a detach
operation should fail. Additionally, one could consider putting the visual servoing loop
around the force/torque controller. This again could confine the forces if the visual ser­
voing is involved in a collision. This is although not expected for the time being, as the
visual servoing is not involved in contact operations.
One could also consider automating the upside down turning of the Print Base. One could
e.g. make a mini station, which could allow the robot to put down the Print Base, turn the
end­effector 180°, and pick it up again. Then the Print Base would automatically have
been put upside down.

==PAGE:105==
Glossary
Tools
The following is a list of tools that are used, with a brief explanation of their purpose.
Ender v3 Pro is a 3D printer.
Fork is a 2­pronged fork tool that is mounted at the end­effector. This is used to move the
Print Base around by locking onto Mount Part B.
Gazebo Robotics simulator that works well with ROS and can simulate physics, cameras,
force/torque sensors, etc.
G­code is a code format with instructions that can be used by a 3D printer. The code
describes the instructions needed to print a specific 3D model.
Manipulator Fork, see Fork.
MeshLab is a open source program that can be used for processing and editing meshes.
Mount Part A is the mount part that is attached to the DLP printer. Fits with Mount Part
B.
Mount Part B is the mount part that is attached to the build plate, and is part of the Print
Base. Fits with Mount Part B and the Fork.
OpenCV is an open source computer vision library for both c, c++ and python.
PolyScope Universal Robots High­level programming interface with features for both new
as well as experienced users [22].
Print Base is the combined Mount Part B and Build Plate (maybe with 3D print as well).
The print base is to be moved around the Soft Tooling Lab, and may therefor be referred
to as the load.
ROS Robot Operating System is a middleware for building robot applications. It contains
many libraries and driver and it allows for a higher level abstraction when programming
robots.
Teach Pendant Universal Robots user interface using PolyScope. For the e­Series robots
it is named 3PE [22].
Soft Tooling Lab is a labaratory at DTU mechanical engineering, where they investigate
the soft tooling process.
SolidWorks is a CAD and CAE program for modeling and simulation of mainly mechani­
cal parts.
Ultimaker Cura is a 3D printer program, that allows for conversion of 3D model files into
G­code. It has setup options for multiple 3D printers, and one can configure the print­
procedure.
Ultimaker 3 Extented is a 3D printer.
Universal Robots ROS Driver is a ROS package that allow for communication and con­
trol of a real or simulated (URSim) robot.
URSim is Universal Robots simulator for offline programming and testing. It runs on a PC
and resembles the PolyScope, but with no real robot connected. It should be the most
behavior realistic simulation available, as long as physics and external sensors are not
needed.
96
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:106==
Expressions
The following is a list of expressions that are used, with a brief explanation of their mean­
ing.
C
Compliant behavior Is when the manipulator allow for some error in it position and veloc­
ity due to forces affecting it. E.g. it is compliant if one can push the end­effector around.
D
Degrees Of Freedom Describes the number of independent parameters that define a
systems configuration or state.
E
End­effector Is the end of the manipulator and may also be known as the tool. In principle
the end­effector is the only part of the robot that may have contact with the environment.
F
Frame Describes a separate coordinate frame, which may have its origin and orientation
a different place. The frames origin/location may be described by a translation and a
rotation in relation to some other frame.
I
IBVS Image­Based Visual Servoing.
J
Joint Position Controller Is the controller which controls the angular position of a single
joint.
Joint Velocity Controller Is the controller which controls the angular velocity of a single
joint.
P
PBVS Position­Based Visual Servoing.
Pose Describes the position (x,y,z) and orientation (roll, pitch, yaw) of an entity in space.
Position Controller Is the controller which controls the end­effector pose (position and
orientation).
Pitch Rotation about the y­axis.
R
Roll Rotation about the z­axis.
T
Twist Is a point’s or object’s velocity described by its linear and angular velocity.
Twist Controller (or velocity controller) Is the controller which controls the end­effector
linear and angular velocity.
V
Velocity controller See twist controller. Must not be confused with joint velocity controller.
Y
Yaw Rotation about the x­axis.

Nomenclature
The following tables contains descriptions of variables used.
Common Operators
a −z
Scalar values are noted with lower case letters.
A −Z
Vectors are noted with capital letters. Exceptions to this are greek letters
like the twist vector ζ.
A −Z
Matrices are noted with bold capital letters (or bold greek letters).
M T
Transpose of M.
M −1
Inverse of M.
M +
Pseudo inverse of M.
|O|
Absolute value(s) of scalar, vector or matrix.
||O||
Length of vector.
I
Identity matrix.
0
Zero vector or matrix of appropriate size.
˙q
The derivative of q.
ˆx
The normalized coordinate of x.
R(r,c)
The element on the (row,column) place of the matrix R.
Skew(O)
Skew symmetric matrix (3x3) calculated based on the translation vector O.
T i
j
Homogeneous transformation matrix (4x4) describing the transform from
the ith to the jth frame.
Oi
j:
Translation vector (3x1) describing the translation from the ith to the jth
frame.
Ri
j:
Rotation matrix (3x3) describing the rotation from the ith to the jth frame.
Rotx(α)
Homogeneous rotation matrix (4x4) for rotation of α about the x­axis.
Roty(ϕ)
Homogeneous rotation matrix (4x4) for rotation of ϕ about the y­axis.
Rotz(θ)
Homogeneous rotation matrix (4x4) for rotation of θ about the z­axis.
T ransx(a)
Homogeneous translation matrix (4x4) for a distance a along the x­axis.
T ransy(b)
Homogeneous translation matrix (4x4) for a distance b along the y­axis.
T ransz(c)
Homogeneous translation matrix (4x4) for a distance c along the z­axis.
cos(α) or cα
Cosine of angle α.
acos(α)
Arccosine (inverse cosine) of angle α.
sin(β) or sβ
Sine of angle β.
tan(ϕ)
Tangent of angle ϕ.
atan2(y, x)
2­argument arctangent, equal to arctan( y
x).
max(K)
Maximum value element of a vector or matrix.
diag(K)
Diagonal matrix with diagonal elements described by the vector K.
Common Symbols
qi
ith joint state variable, as all joints are revolute qi = θi.
θi
ith joint angle.
x, y, z
3D Cartesian coordinates.
u, v
Image plane coordinates.
P
A point vector.
˜P
A homogeneous point vector based on P.
R
3x3 Rotation matrix.
.
O
3x1 Translation vector.
T
4x4 Transformation matrix.
ζ
6x1 twist vector, comprised of linear and angular velocities.
F
6x1 wrench vector, comprised of linear and rotational forces.
f
Focal length.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
99
==PAGE:109==
Image Processing Symbols
A
Intrinsic parameter matrix.
ku, kv
Pixel scaling factors (for camera calibration).
k1, k2, k3
Radial distortion coefficients.
p1, p2
Tangential distortion coefficients.
u0, v0
Image plane coordinate offsets (for camera calibration).
ud, vd
Distorted image plane coordinates.
PID Control Symbols
kp
Proportional gain of PID controller.
ki
Integral gain of PID controller.
kd
Derivative gain of PID controller.
e
Error signal.
Kinematics Symbols
ai
Link length (DH­parameter).
di
Link offset (DH­parameter).
αi
Link twist (DH­parameter).
Gi
Homogeneous transformation matrix describing the transform from the
ith −1 to the ith frame.
H
Homogeneous transformation matrix describing the entire transform from
the first (0th) to the last (nth) frame.
J
The Jacobian matrix.
JV
The linear velocity sub­matrix of the Jacobian.
Jω
The angular velocity sub­matrix of the Jacobian.
Visual Servoing Symbols
E
The feature error vector.
L
The image Jacobian.
S
Image feature positions (x1,y1,x2...yn) vector.
˙S
Velocity of image features.
Sgoal
Goal image feature positions (x1,y1,x2...yn) vector.
vin, vout, vmax
Velocities input, output, and maximum (limit).
λ
Positive gain matrix. Usually λ = kλI.
γ
Gamma mapping variable (scalar).
Force/Torque Control Symbols
Bd
The desired damping matrix.
Md
Desired inertia matrix.
s
Laplacian operator.
tsample
Sample time.
z
Z­domain operator.

The angular velocities of the joint when commanded by the IVK algorithm to
move in a circle. The simulator output (Actual) and the reference calcu­
lated offline (Theo. Ref.) both nicely follows the reference calculated for
the simulator (Gazebo Ref.). The purely theoretical, that has the twist com­
mand updated every 4th time sample, is following closely but deviates as
the actual period time is larger than 4 samples. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 55

almost does not move, as desired, and the y and z follow sine or cosine
curves to make a circular movement combined. The increasing amplitude
of the curves results in a spiral movement. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 56

Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:124==
E
Camera Calibration & Marker Detection
E.1
Camera Calibration Procedure
In order to use the FLIR camera one has to install the camera spinnaker software onto
the computer. This software can be downloaded for Ubuntu 18.04 from Teledyne FLIR.
Additionally the Python package for Python 2.7 was downloaded (as ROS does not sup­
port Python 3). Both can be installed using the guides in the read­me files. Additionally
one should install PySpin, which can be done through the package installer for Python
(pip). By using the Python code example AcquireAndDisplay.py one can create a script
for acquiring images directly into the script which can be processed with OpenCV.
The following describe the developed camera calibration Python script. The code of this
script can also be seen in E.2.
On figure E.1 the flowchart of the cam­
era calibration Python script can be
seen.
Initially there is declarations of
settings like checkerboard size (7x9),
empty point vectors and the static world
coordinates of the checkerboard. Next
each image is loaded into the program
and processed.
The process search
for checkerboard corners, and if found,
improves the corner point estimates.
These points are added to a vector of
2D points, and as the checkerboard is
considered static, the static world coor­
dinates are simply added to a vector of
3D points. Lastly the corners are drawn
onto the image, such that visual inspec­
tion and verification can be performed.
If all seems okay, the next image is pro­
cessed. This continues through all the
calibration images.
Lastly the actual camera calibration is
performed using the 2D and 3D vector
containing the points of all the images.
Figure E.1: Flowchart for the camera
calibration script written in Python.
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
115
==PAGE:125==
E.2
Python Scrip: Camera Calibration
1
import os
2
import cv2

4

import numpy as np

7

# Checkerboard dimensions
11
CHECKERBOARD = (7, 9)
12
13
# Stop criteria: accuracy + epsilon is reached or specified number of
iterations are completed.
14
criteria = (cv2.TERM_CRITERIA_EPS + cv2.TERM_CRITERIA_MAX_ITER , 30, 0.001)
15
16
17
# Vector for 3D points
18
points3D = []
19
20
# Vector for 2D points
21
points2D = []
22

# 3D points real world coordinates
25
objectp3d = np.zeros((1, CHECKERBOARD[0]*CHECKERBOARD[1], 3), np.float32)
26
objectp3d[0, :, :2] = np.mgrid[0:CHECKERBOARD[0], 0:CHECKERBOARD[1]].T.reshape
(-1, 2)
27
prev_img_shape = None
28
29
30
# Path of images:
31
imageFiles = glob.glob('FLIRImages/*.jpg')
32
print("Images Found:")
33
print(imageFiles)
34
35
i = 0
36
img_shape = 0
37
#For each image
38
for file in imageFiles:
39
image = cv2.imread(file)
40
img_gray = cv2.cvtColor(image, cv2.COLOR_BGR2GRAY)
41

F.2
Moment of Inertia Tensors
The following sections contains the code for calculating the inertia tensors and for printing
them via MATLAB.
F.2.1
Inertia_Calc.m
Inertia_Calc.m contains the code for calculating and printing the moment of inertia tensors
for each of the simulated components. It specifies the sizes of the objects, and whether
if they should be calculated using a cylinder or cuboid approximation. As can be seen
from the code, the Mount Part B is combined of 6 different cuboids, and each will have its
own moment of inertia tensor. The base also has the weight of the metal plate, while the
rest of the component share the weight of the mount part B depending on their individual
volume.
1
I_cuboid = @(X,Y,Z) [1/12*(Y^2+Z^2) 0 0 ; 0 1/12*(X^2+Z^2) 0 ; 0 0 1/12*(X^2 +
Y^2)];
2
I_cylinder = @(r,Z) [1/12*(3*r^2+Z^2) 0 0 ; 0 1/12*(3*r^2+Z^2) 0 ; 0 0 1/2*r
^2];
3
4
5

F.3
ROS­Gazebo Joint Controller Setup
The following sections will go through the steps of setting up the custom joint controllers for
ROS and Gazebo. The overall process can be described with the flowchart on figure F.1.
As can be seen, first the ros_control package is needed, then one setup the controllers
config file, the transmissions and spawn the controllers from the roslaunch­file.
Figure F.1: Flowchart for setting up a ros_control controller for Gazebo and ROS.
F.3.1
Setting the Hardware interface
The effort_controllers uses the EffortJointInterface hardware interface, which is used to
send and receive commands to the hardware. Gazebo can tab into this interface by
specifying the transmissions of the joints of the robot URDF model. Modifying the UR5e
model can be done by changing the hardwareInterface options of all the joints in the
<...>.transmissions.xacro file to:
1
<hardwareInterface>hardware_interface/EffortJointInterface</
hardwareInterface>
F.3.2
Specifying the Controllers
Additionally one must specify a controller which should be used. This is done by creating
a controller .yaml file. Here it is named ur5e_jnt_vel_pos_ctrl.yaml, and can be found in
appendix F.4. In this file the joint state controller is declared, together with a position and
a velocity controller for each of the joint. The naming convention follows that of the robot
URDF file (ur5e.urdf.xacro). The controller names are:
• shoulder_pan_joint_velocity_controller
• shoulder_lift_joint_velocity_controller
• elbow_joint_velocity_controller
• wrist_1_joint_velocity_controller
• wrist_2_joint_velocity_controller
• wrist_3_joint_velocity_controller
• shoulder_pan_joint_position_controller
• shoulder_lift_joint_position_controller
• elbow_joint_position_controller
• wrist_1_joint_position_controller
• wrist_2_joint_position_controller
• wrist_3_joint_position_controller
The controllers specify the types: effort_controllers\JointVelocityController
or effort_controllers\JointPositionController, the joint name (same as controller but without
_..._controller) and lastly the PID gains of each controller. The tuning of these gains is
described in section 5.3.
124
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing

Backmatter/Code/ur5e_jnt_vel_pos_ctrl.yaml
F.4.1
Launching the Controllers
To launch the controllers one must specify them in the appropriate launch file. Such
a launch file can be seen in appendix F.5. Here the controller file described above is
included and loaded. From this the controllers are loaded using the controller_spawner
from the controller_manager package. The joint position controllers and the joint velocity
controllers cannot be running at the same time, as this would result in control conflicts.
Therefore the joint position controller is initially loaded, and the joint velocity controllers
are loaded with a –stopped parameter. This allows for controller switching later, via the
controller manager. Lastly a robot_state_publisher is created to publish the robot states
to a ROS topic.
Apart from this, the launch file also contains the necessary code for starting Gazebo with
a world and spawning the UR5e robot.
F.4.2
Using the Controllers from ROS
With the controllers loaded, one can control the individual joints by simply publishing ref­
erence values to the appropriate command topic. E.g. one can publish references to the
should pan joint using the following python code:
1
#Creating publishers:
2
pSP_vel = rospy.Publisher("/shoulder_pan_joint_velocity_controller/command",
Float64 , queue_size=1)
3
pSP_pos = rospy.Publisher("/shoulder_pan_joint_position_controller/command",
Float64 , queue_size=1)
4
#...
5
#Publish a joint angle position:
6
pSP_pos.publish(Float64(JntPos))
7
8
#Switch controller:
9
rospy.wait_for_service('/controller_manager/switch_controller')
10
switch_controller = rospy.ServiceProxy('/controller_manager/switch_controller'
, SwitchController)
11
switch_controller(['shoulder_pan_joint_velocity_controller'], ['
shoulder_pan_joint_position_controller'], 2, False, 0.0)
12
#Publish a joint angle position:
13
pSP_vel.publish(Float64(JntSpeed))
Here the controller is also switched from a position controller to a velocity controller.
126
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing

F.5
Gazebo Launch Scrip: The ROS­Gazebo Joint Controllers
and Lab

<?xml version="1.0"?>
2
<launch >
3
<arg name="limited" default="true" doc="If true, limits joint range [-PI, PI
] on all joints." />
4
<arg name="paused" default="false" doc="Starts gazebo in paused mode" />
5
<arg name="gui" default="true" doc="Starts gazebo gui" />
6
<arg name="transmission_hw_interface" default='hardware_interface/
PositionJointInterface'/>
7
8
<!-- startup simulated world -->
9
<include file="$(find gazebo_ros)/launch/empty_world.launch">
10
<arg name="world_name" default="$(find stl_ur5e_controller)/worlds/
SoftToolingLab.world"/>
11
<arg name="paused" value="$(arg paused)"/>
12
<arg name="gui" value="$(arg gui)"/>
13
</include >
14
15
<!--<arg name="gazebo_world_file" default="$(find stl_ur5e_controller)/
worlds/SoftToolinLab.world"/> Changes from "worlds/empty.world"-->
16
17
18
<param name="robot_description" command="$(find xacro)/xacro '$(find
stl_ur5e_controller)/urdf/stl_ur5e.urdf.xacro'"/>
19
20
<!-- push robot description to factory and spawn robot in gazebo -->
21
<node name="spawn_gazebo_model" pkg="gazebo_ros" type="spawn_model" args="-
urdf -param robot_description -model robot -z 0.0" respawn="false"
output="screen"/>
22
23
24
<!-- THE VEL POS CONTROLLER: -->
25
<!-- Load joint controller configurations from YAML file to parameter server
-->
26
<rosparam file="$(find stl_ur5e_controller)/config/ur5e_jnt_vel_pos_ctrl.
yaml" command="load"/>
27
28
<!-- load the controllers -->
29
<node name="controller_spawner" pkg="controller_manager" type="spawner"
respawn="false" output="screen" args="
shoulder_pan_joint_position_controller
shoulder_lift_joint_position_controller elbow_joint_position_controller
wrist_1_joint_position_controller wrist_2_joint_position_controller
wrist_3_joint_position_controller joint_state_controller"/>
30
31
<node name="controller_spawner_stopped" pkg="controller_manager" type="
spawner" respawn="false" output="screen" args="--stopped
shoulder_pan_joint_velocity_controller
shoulder_lift_joint_velocity_controller elbow_joint_velocity_controller
wrist_1_joint_velocity_controller wrist_2_joint_velocity_controller
wrist_3_joint_velocity_controller"/>
32
33
<!-- convert joint states to TF transforms for rviz, etc -->
34
<node name="robot_state_publisher2" pkg="robot_state_publisher" type="
robot_state_publisher" respawn="false" output="screen">
35
<remap from="/joint_states" to="/ur5e/joint_states" />
36
</node>
37
38
</launch>

G
Digital Twin: Joint Position and
Velocity Controller Tuning
G.1
Acquiring ROS­Gazebo PID Tuning Data
Data to evaluate the performance of the designed controller, could be extracted by reading
the controller state topics (e.g. elbow_joint_position_controller_state). These contain the
information of the set point (reference), error, command value and the process value (see
figure 5.3). Reading the topics and storing the data, can be done by using a tool called
rqt_gui. This has the option of recording specific topics in a ROS Bag. This bag can later
be cut into independent csv data files for each topic or subtopic by using the terminal
command:
1
rostopic echo -b <BagName >.bag -p /<ControllerTopicName >/state/<ValueName > > <
CsvFileName >.txt
A slightly different approach was used in the second tuning. Here the rqt_gui plot option
was used to show live signal and responses of the controller, while the controller could be
tuned live using the tool option Dynamic Reconfigure. Additionally it was discovered that
the rosbag could be saved directly through the terminal using the command below, which
gave an easier and more robust way of capturing data.
1
rostopic echo -b <BagName >.bag -p rosbag record -O data
shoulder_pan_joint_velocity_controller/state
shoulder_lift_joint_velocity_controller/state
elbow_joint_velocity_controller/state wrist_1_joint_velocity_controller/
state wrist_2_joint_velocity_controller/state
wrist_3_joint_velocity_controller/state /joint_states
G.2
UR5e ROS­Gazebo PID Tuning
This subsection presents plots related to the tuning of the ROS­Gazebo PID controllers
for the UR5e robot model. Both the initial controller plots as well as the final controllers
are presented, for both joint velocity and joint position.
G.2.1
Tuning the First Joint Position Controller
The initial joint position controller gains presented in table G.1 does not yield desireable
controller performance, as can also be seen from figure G.1, G.2 and G.3. Here it is clear
that the Shoulder Lift Joint and Wrist 3 Joint does not perform as indented, due to the
undershoot. Additionally, as the arm joints are highly coupled systems, this individual test
does not give a full picture of the joint behavior. This is the reason why the Wrist 2 Joint
controller also have been re­tuned, as it showed undesirable behavior during tests with
multiple joint movements.
Compared to the initial controller, the shoulder lift joint gain has been increased, to ensure
sufficient effort when working against gravity. The differential gain of Wrist 2 have been
removed, and the integral gain instead increased, resulting in a PI controller. This helped
remove the undesired oscillatory behavior seen with the initial values. The wrist 1 has
also lost its differential gain, and the proportional gain has been decreased. The gains
128
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:138==
can be see in table G.2. The resulting step response for a change in reference of ±1 rad
can be seen on figure G.4, G.6 and G.5. As it can be seen all joints quickly reach their
reference position with the error going to zero.
The tuning of the second joint position controller is covered in section 5.3.1.
G.2.2
Initial Joint Position Controller Plots
Below the step response of each initial joint position controller can be found. These re­
sponses are made to a change in reference of 1­1.57 rad. The first figure (G.1) shows
the joint angular position (output of system) together with the reference, the second (G.2)
shows the command (effort) (output of controller), and the last (G.3) shows the error sig­
nal. The PID gains for this controller was found by [48] for a UR5 with no tool (not UR5e).
The PID gains used for these plots are as can be seen of table G.1.

G.2.4
Second Joint Position Controller Plots
Similar to the other joint position controller test, tests and plots can be created for the
final controller. These responses are made to a change in reference of 1.57 rad (0.8 for
shoulder lift due to logistics). Here the plots for the test without load is presented together
with the command signal of the one with load.
The no load plots: The first figure shows the joint angular position (output of system)
together with the reference (figure G.7), the second shows the command (effort) (output
of controller) (figure G.8), and the last shows the error signal (figure G.9).
The command signal plot for the controller with load is on figure G.10.
The PID gains for this controller was found by hand tuning as described in subsection
5.3.1, and can be seen in table 5.5.

seen in table 5.6.

together with the reference (figure G.17), the second shows the command (effort) (output
of controller) (figure G.18), and the last shows the error signal(figure G.19).

H
System Communication
H.1
Installation of URSim
The installation of the URSim for Linux can be found at [61]. As the simulator have been
tested with a Linux Mint 17.1, such a Linux distribution have been installed onto an old
computer, which will work as robot test simulator.
After this, the simulator can be downloaded and installed. In this regard some trouble
was encountered when installing the software. To fix this, first of all Python 2.6 should be
installed, and secondly one must have java version between 6 and 10 installed. When
both of these are satisfied the installation could be proceeded like described at [61].
Later an Ubuntu 18.04 version was used for running URSim instead (The mint version
could still have been used).
H.2
Setting up URSim / UR5e for Driver Control
To be able to communicate and perform external control of the URSim and/or the UR5e
robot, one must install a URCap program. This is done in the exact same way for both
the simulator and the real robot, and the guide of [62] can be used. Here one download
the URCap program and transfers it by USB. One can then setup the IP­address of the
external ROS­computer and the custom port to be used.
H.3
Installing the Universal Robots ROS Driver
In order to control the real robot and the URsim robot, Universal_Robots_ROS_Driver
ROS driver for the e­Series robots has to be installed. This can be found at [18], where a
guide for installation can also be found. The following sections will give some notes and
advice in this installation process, as it is not always easy to follow.
H.3.1
Installing Real Time Kernel for Ubuntu
The driver highly recommends using a real time kernel for the system that is to control the
robot arm. This is to robustly accommodate the 2ms cycle time of the e­Series robots[18],
which is higher than the older models. The guide recommends to compile a kernel with a
PREEMPT_RT patch onto it.
A guide for installing the kernel can be found at [63]. The system used before installation
was an Ubuntu 18.04 with a 2.15.0­176­generic kernel installed. This kernel does not
have an actively supported real time kernel patch, and thence the kernel should be either
down or upgraded. In this case the easiest is to choose a kernel from the 2.14­versions
and a matching rt­patch. Here a linux kernel 2.14.275 and matching rt­patch 2.14.275­
rt132 has been chosen.
Unfortunately the guide could only be followed until the compilation of the kernel and
patch. In this step the compilation returned an error:
recipe for target 'deb-pkg' failed
make: *** [deb-pkg] Error 2
This error may occur when compiling to a debian package, and a suggested solution was
to follow a compiling guide not using this approach [64]. Instead partly following the old
guide and a new guide [65] the following install steps where performed in the terminal
from the kernel install folder:
1
#Downloading the kernel and the patch
142
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:152==

$ wget https://www.kernel.org/pub/linux/kernel/v4.x/linux -4.14.275.tar.xz

$ wget https://cdn.kernel.org/pub/linux/kernel/projects/rt/4.14/patch
-4.14.275-rt132.patch.gz

After this, the steps of the old guide, for setting up the GRUB to booting the real­time
kernel can be followed. When restarting the computer, an error may occur:
Loading initial ramdisk error: you need to load the kernel first
This may be fixed by disabling secure boot on the computer.

H.4
Network connections
To create a network communication between the ROS host and the URSim or UR5e robot,
one can connect using an Ethernet cable with proper settings.
URSim: This URSim run on an Ubuntu 18.04 same as the ROS host. For both computers
one can go to the cabled network setting. One can then add a connection configuration
where the IPv4 settings are set to ”local only”. From here one can use the ifconfig
command in terminal to see the Ethernet IP addresses of both computers. These IP ad­
dresses are later used for connecting via the URCap in the URSim and from the terminal
command that boots the UR ROS driver form the ROS host.
UR5e: On the UR5e robot teach pendant, one can go to the settings using the burger
menu. From here one can find the network settings showing both IP address and gate
(these are set to static). Here these are 192.168.0.2, 255.255.255.0. From the ROS Host
one can create another network configuration, using static IP addresses in the IPv4 tab,
setting this to 192.168.0.1, 255.255.255.0. Rest is left as is. In the IPv6 tab one can dis­
able the use of the IPv6. IP addresses can be verified by using ping <IP Address> and
ifconfig.
H.4.1
Using the driver
The UR ROS driver can after installation and network setup easily be used by running:
roslaunch ur_robot_driver ur5e_bringup.launch robot_ip:=<URSim/UR5e IP as found
above>
This runs the UR5e launch file, which holds the settings for the driver. The driver initiates
a number of controllers that may be used to control the robot, and the setting of these
can be found in that launch file. Additionally testing the controller can be done, using the
test_move script that comes with the driver.
Modifying the launch file and using the example code, the driver can be setup to initiate
a Twist controller (twist_controller) and a position trajectory controller
(forward_cartesian_traj_controller). Initially joint_based_cartesian_traj_controller was used,
but there is an error with this controller leading to nonoptimal trajectory and joint solutions.
The controller may not use the solution with the smallest joint angle changes, and hence
144
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:154==
might change configuration (e.g. elbow­up/elbow­down etc.) even when commanding
small pose changes. This was initially a great issue, as the position commands could not
always be trusted to behave rationally. Instead using forward_cartesian_traj_controller
solved the issue.
These controllers combined give the same functionality as the joint velocity and joint po­
sition controllers does, when using the kinematic equations of the robot.
Using this, one can now run the scrips connected either to the URSim for behavior verifi­
cation, or connecting to the real UR5e Robot.
Using the real robot, one may first like to get the robot calibration file, and use this as well
when booting the driver. One can get the calibration by using:
$ roslaunch ur_calibration calibration_correction.launch
robot_ip:=192.168.0.2
target_filename:="$HOME/my_robot_calibration.yaml"
Then use this calibration file when booting the driver:
$ roslaunch ur_robot_driver ur5e_bringup.launch robot_ip:=192.168.0.1
kinematics_config:=<path to calibration file>

real camera), IBVS.py, UR5eFK.py, UR5eIK.py (for Gazebo) and URDriverControl.py (for
Real setup).

201

202
#
203

*** LATER ***
204

205

def ImageJacobian(x,y,Z):
196

[0, -1/Z, y/Z, (1 + y**2), -x*y, -x] ])

def GetVSTwist(imgPos, seachID , lam):

[ferror[3,0]], [ferror[3,1]] ])

def TwistMaxTransform(twist):

def TwistGammaTransform(twist):

def AdjointTransform(twistCmd_cam , Tb6):
296
#Rotation matrix form end effector to camera
297
R6cam = np.array([ [math.cos(-pi/2), -math.sin(-pi/2), 0],\
298
[math.sin(-pi/2), math.cos(-pi/2), 0],\
299
[0, 0, 1] ])

302
[0.045, 0, 0],\
303
[-0.058, 0, 0] ])

305
Ad1 = np.column_stack(( R6cam, np.matmul(S_d,R6cam) ))
306
Ad2 = np.column_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), R6cam ))
307
AdT = np.row_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
308
#Transform the camera frame twist to end effector frame
309

311
Rb6 = Tb6[0:3,0:3]
312

313

314
AdT = np.row_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
315
#Transform end effector frame twist to base frame
316
twistCmd_b = np.matmul(AdT, twist66)
317
return twistCmd_b

def IKinematics(pose):
170
# INVERSE KINEMATICS OF UR5e Take 2
171
# pose = [x, y, z, r, p, y]
172
err = False
173
174
#The Goal Position becomes:
175
#H = [R, o ; 0, 1]
176
R = np.matmul(get_kin_Rb0(), R_ZYX(pose[3], pose[4], pose[5])) #T_R
(1:3,1:3)
177
#print(R)
178
o = np.array([ [-pose[0]], [-pose[1]], [pose[2]] ]) #Sign change due to
rotation of frame
179
180
a = get_kin_a()
181
d = get_kin_d()
182
alpha = get_kin_alpha()
183
184
#Based on UR5 IK (UR5 Inverse Kinematics M.E. 530.646, Ryan Keating @ John
185
#Hopkins University)
186
T0_6 = np.row_stack( (np.column_stack((R,o)), np.array([0,0,0,1])))
187
print('The Goal Transformation Becomes:')
188
print(T0_6)
189
P0_5 = np.matmul(T0_6, np.array([ [0], [0], [-d[5]], [1] ])) - np.array([
[0], [0], [0], [1] ])
190
191
192
#Theta 1:
193
psi = math.atan2(P0_5[1], P0_5[0])
194
phi = math.acos( d[3]/math.sqrt(P0_5[0]**2 + P0_5[1]**2) )
195
#phi = -acos( d(4)/sqrt(P0_5(1)^2 + P0_5(2)^2 ) );
196
theta_1 = psi + phi + pi/2
197
198
if( d[3] > math.sqrt( P0_5[0]**2 + P0_5[1]**2 ) ):
199
print('NB eqn 5 does not have a solution!')
200
err = True
201
202
203
#Theta 5:
204
P0_6 = np.row_stack( (o, np.array([1])))
205
P1_6_z = P0_6[0]*math.sin(theta_1)-P0_6[1]*math.cos(theta_1)
206
theta_5 =
math.acos( (P1_6_z-d[3])/d[5] )
207
#theta_5 = -acos( (P1_6_z -d(4))/d(6) )
208
209
210
#Theta_6
211
pos = np.array([theta_1 , 0, 0, 0, theta_5 , 0])
212
T0_1 = T(pos, 1)
213
T1_6 = np.matmul(np.linalg.inv(T0_1), T0_6)
214
T6_1 = np.linalg.inv(T1_6)
215
z_x = T6_1[0,2]; z_y = T6_1[1,2];
216
theta_6 = math.atan2(-z_y/math.sin(theta_5), z_x/math.sin(theta_5))
217
218
219
#The remaining:
220
pos = np.array([theta_1 , 0, 0, 0, theta_5 , theta_6])
221
T4_5 = T(pos, 5)
222
T5_6 = T(pos, 6)
223
T1_4 = np.matmul(T1_6, np.linalg.inv(np.matmul(T4_5,T5_6)))
224
P1_3 = np.matmul(T1_4, np.array([ [0], [-d[3]], [0], [1]])) - np.array([
[0], [0], [0], [1]])
170
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:180==
225
226
#theta_3
227
arg = (np.linalg.norm(P1_3)**2 - a[1]**2 - a[2]**2)/( 2*a[1]*a[2]);
228
theta_3 =
math.acos( arg )
229
#theta_3 = -math.acos( arg )
230

if( (arg > 1) or (arg < -1) ):

print('ERROR! Target is out of reach!')

err = True

#theta_2
237

)/np.linalg.norm(P1_3) )
238

#theta_4

a,d,alpha, pos, 4)*T(a,d,alpha, pos, 5)*T(a,d,alpha, pos, 6)

make it possible to run the visual servoing and force/torque controller for both Gazebo and
UR5e. The following control scripts are in this section: VisualServoingController.py (for

Gazebo), FT_Controller_Tester_Real.py (for real setup),

def EEAdjointTransform(vel_EE , Tb6):
85
Rb6 = Tb6[0:3,0:3]
86
Ad1 = np.row_stack(( Rb6, np.zeros((3,3)) ))
87
Ad2 = np.row_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), Rb6 ))
88
AdT = np.column_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
89
90
#Transform end-effector frame twist to base frame
91
vel_b = np.matmul(AdT, vel_EE)
92
return vel_b

def CalcTF(e0, e1, u1, b0, b1, a1):
282
u0 = b0*e0 + b1*e1 - a1*u1
283
return u0

def ForceTorqueControl100Hz(Fd, Vd):
288
global currentFT , u, e, FTBias
289
sample = AdjointTransformFTSensor(currentFT - FTBias , UR5eFK.get_kin_T06(
URDriverControl.GetJointStateOrdered()))
290
e[0,:] = Fd - sample
291
WriteFTValues(sample)
292
293
#Forces:
294
#Normal , 5x Mass, 5x Damping , 0.2xMass, 0.2xDamping , 0.5xDamping + 0.1xMass
, 1xDamping + 0.1xMass,
295
b0 = 0.0005556#From Md and Bd
296
b1 = 0.0005556
297
a0 = 1
298
a1 = 0.7778
299
u[0,0] = CalcTF(e[0,0], e[1,0], u[1,0], b0, b1, a1)
300
u[0,1] = CalcTF(e[0,1], e[1,1], u[1,1], b0, b1, a1)
301
u[0,2] = CalcTF(e[0,2], e[1,2], u[1,2], b0, b1, a1)
302
303
#Torques:
304
b0 = 0.0125 #From Md and Bd
305
b1 = 0.0125
306
a0 = 1
307
a1 = 0.5
308
u[0,3] = CalcTF(e[0,3], e[1,3], u[1,3], b0, b1, a1)
309
u[0,4] = CalcTF(e[0,4], e[1,4], u[1,4], b0, b1, a1)
310
u[0,5] = CalcTF(e[0,5], e[1,5], u[1,5], b0, b1, a1)
311
312
u[1,:] = u[0,:] #Shift
313
e[1,:] = e[0,:] #Shift
314
315
velOut = np.array(Vd-u[0,:])
316
return velOut

def twistLimit(twist, linLim, angLim):
321
for i in range(0,3):
322
if((twist[i] > linLim) or (twist[i] < -linLim)):
323
twist[i] = linLim*np.sign(twist[i])
324
325
for i in range(3,6):
326
if((twist[i] > angLim) or (twist[i] < -angLim)):
327
twist[i] = angLim*np.sign(twist[i])
328
329
return np.array(twist)

def AdjointTransformFTSensor(FT_ft, Tb6):
334
FT_ft = [FT_ft[0], FT_ft[1], FT_ft[2], 0, 0, FT_ft[5]*2]
335
336
#Rotation matrix form end effector to FT sensor
337
R6ft = UR5eFK.R_y(0)
338
339
#Screw symmetric matrix
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
185
==PAGE:195==
340
S_d = np.array([ [0, 0, 0],\
341
[0, 0, 0],\
342
[0, 0, 0] ])
343
344
Ad1 = np.row_stack(( R6ft, np.matmul(S_d,R6ft) ))
345
Ad2 = np.row_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), R6ft ))
346
AdT = np.column_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
347
#Transform the sensor frame wrench to end effector frame
348
FT_66 = np.matmul(AdT, FT_ft)
349
350
Rb6 = Tb6[0:3,0:3]
351
Ad1 = np.row_stack(( Rb6, np.zeros((3,3)) ))
352
Ad2 = np.row_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), Rb6 ))
353
AdT = np.column_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
354
#Transform end-effector frame wrench to base frame
355
FT_b = np.matmul(AdT, FT_66)
356
return FT_b

def CalcTF(e0, e1, u1, b0, b1, a1):
44
u0 = b0*e0 + b1*e1 - a1*u1
45
return u0

def twistLimit(twist, linLim, angLim):
140
for i in range(0,3):
141
if((twist[i] > linLim) or (twist[i] < -linLim)):
142
twist[i] = linLim*np.sign(twist[i])
143
144
for i in range(3,6):
145
if((twist[i] > angLim) or (twist[i] < -angLim)):
146
twist[i] = angLim*np.sign(twist[i])
147
148
return np.array(twist)

def AdjointTransformFTSensor(FT_ft, Tb6):
153
global pi
154
FT_ft = [FT_ft[0], FT_ft[1], FT_ft[2], FT_ft[3], 0, 0]
155
R6ft = UR5eFK.R_ZYX(pi, -pi/2, 0)
156
157
#Screw symmetric matrix
158
S_d = np.array([ [0, -0.01, 0],\
159
[0.01, 0, 0],\
160
[0, 0, 0] ])
161
162
Ad1 = np.column_stack(( R6ft, np.matmul(S_d,R6ft) ))
163
Ad2 = np.column_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), R6ft ))
164
AdT = np.row_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
165
#Transform the sensor frame wrench to end effector frame
166
FT_66 = np.matmul(AdT, FT_ft)
167
168
Rb6 = Tb6[0:3,0:3]
169
Ad1 = np.column_stack(( Rb6, np.zeros((3,3)) ))
170
Ad2 = np.column_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), Rb6 ))
171
AdT = np.row_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
192
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:202==
172
#Transform end-effector frame wrench to base frame
173
FT_b = np.matmul(AdT, FT_66)
174
return FT_b

def EEAdjointTransform(vel_EE , Tb6):
83

def EEMoveVel(velocity , duration):

def CalcTF(e0, e1, u1, b0, b1, a1):
266
u0 = b0*e0 + b1*e1 - a1*u1
267
return u0

def twistLimit(twist, linLim, angLim):
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
201
==PAGE:211==
341
for i in range(0,3):
342
if((twist[i] > linLim) or (twist[i] < -linLim)):
343
twist[i] = linLim*np.sign(twist[i])
344
345
for i in range(3,6):
346
if((twist[i] > angLim) or (twist[i] < -angLim)):
347
twist[i] = angLim*np.sign(twist[i])
348
349
return np.array(twist)

def AdjointTransformFTSensor(FT_ft, Tb6):
354
FT_ft = [FT_ft[0], FT_ft[1], FT_ft[2], 0, 0, FT_ft[5]*2]
355
356
#Rotation matrix form end effector to FT sensor
357
R6ft = UR5eFK.R_y(0)
358
359
#Screw symmetric matrix
360
S_d = np.array([ [0, 0, 0],\
361
[0, 0, 0],\
362
[0, 0, 0] ])
363
364
Ad1 = np.row_stack(( R6ft, np.matmul(S_d,R6ft) ))
365
Ad2 = np.row_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), R6ft ))
366
AdT = np.column_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
367
#Transform the sensor frame wrench to end effector frame
368
FT_66 = np.matmul(AdT, FT_ft)
369
370
Rb6 = Tb6[0:3,0:3]
371
Ad1 = np.row_stack(( Rb6, np.zeros((3,3)) ))
372
Ad2 = np.row_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), Rb6 ))
373
AdT = np.column_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
374
#Transform end-effector frame wrench to base frame
375
FT_b = np.matmul(AdT, FT_66)
376
return FT_b

def EEAdjointTransform(vel_EE, Tb6):
84
Rb6 = Tb6[0:3,0:3]
85
Ad1 = np.row_stack(( Rb6, np.zeros((3,3)) ))
86
Ad2 = np.row_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), Rb6 ))
87
AdT = np.column_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
88
89
#Transform end-effector frame twist to base frame
90
vel_b = np.matmul(AdT, vel_EE)
91
return vel_b

def CalcTF(e0, e1, u1, b0, b1, a1):
268
u0 = b0*e0 + b1*e1 - a1*u1
269
return u0

def ForceTorqueControl100Hz(Fd, Vd):

Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing

==PAGE:221==

#Forces:
280
#Normal , 5x Mass, 5x Damping , 0.2xMass, 0.2xDamping , 0.5xDamping + 0.1xMass
, 1xDamping + 0.1xMass,
281
b0 = 0.0005556#From Md and Bd
282
b1 = 0.0005556
283
a0 = 1

a1 = 0.7778

u[0,0] = CalcTF(e[0,0], e[1,0], u[1,0], b0, b1, a1)

287
u[0,2] = CalcTF(e[0,2], e[1,2], u[1,2], b0, b1, a1)
288

#Torques:
290
b0 = 0.0125 #From Md and Bd

b1 = 0.0125
292
a0 = 1

a1 = 0.5

u[0,4] = CalcTF(e[0,4], e[1,4], u[1,4], b0, b1, a1)

297
298
u[1,:] = u[0,:] #Shift
299
e[1,:] = e[0,:] #Shift
300
301
velOut = np.array(Vd-u[0,:])
302
return velOut

def twistLimit(twist, linLim, angLim):
307
for i in range(0,3):
308
if((twist[i] > linLim) or (twist[i] < -linLim)):
309
twist[i] = linLim*np.sign(twist[i])
310
311
for i in range(3,6):
312
if((twist[i] > angLim) or (twist[i] < -angLim)):
313
twist[i] = angLim*np.sign(twist[i])
314
315
return np.array(twist)

def AdjointTransformFTSensor(FT_ft, Tb6):
320
FT_ft = [FT_ft[0], FT_ft[1], FT_ft[2], 0, 0, FT_ft[5]*2]
321
322
#Rotation matrix form end-effector to FT sensor
323
R6ft = UR5eFK.R_y(0)
324
325
#Screw symmetric matrix
326
S_d = np.array([ [0, 0, 0],\
327
[0, 0, 0],\
328
[0, 0, 0] ])
329
330
Ad1 = np.column_stack(( R6ft, np.matmul(S_d,R6ft) ))
331
Ad2 = np.column_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), R6ft ))
332
AdT = np.row_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
333
#Transform the sensor frame wrench to end effector frame
334
FT_66 = np.matmul(AdT, FT_ft)
335
336
Rb6 = Tb6[0:3,0:3]
337
Ad1 = np.column_stack(( Rb6, np.zeros((3,3)) ))
338
Ad2 = np.column_stack(( np.zeros((3,3)), Rb6 ))
339
AdT = np.row_stack(( Ad1, Ad2))
212
Visual Force/Torque Hybrid Control for High­Accuracy Manipulation in DLP Printing
==PAGE:222==
340
#Transform end-effector frame wrench to base frame
341
FT_b = np.matmul(AdT, FT_66)
342
return FT_b

def ForceTorqueController(event=None):

if(time_old == 0):

355
ct = time_new -time_old
356
WriteCycleTimeValues(ct)
357
CycleTime.append(ct)

359

360

0.5 for freedrive testing
361
twistCmd_b = [twistCmd_b[0], twistCmd_b[1], twistCmd_b[2], twistCmd_b
[3], twistCmd_b[4], twistCmd_b[5]] #Disables torque control

URDriverControl.TwistCommand(twistCmd_b)
